[F41] WEAPONS -- F41 covers inventions related to weapons and weaponry, encompassing firearms, artillery, missile systems, ammunition, and associated accessories or mechanisms for launching, firing, or controlling projectiles. The class also includes protective devices and methods of use for military or civilian applications.
[F41A] FUNCTIONAL FEATURES OR DETAILS COMMON TO BOTH SMALLARMS AND ORDNANCE, e.g. CANNONS -- **Definition:** This place covers:  This subclass covers those features or details which are considered to be of a kind generally applicable to, or to be concerned with intrinsic functions common to, both smallarms and ordnance. Such features or details are classified in this subclass, even if they are stated to be applied only to smallarms or only to ordnance.
[F41A 1/00] Missile propulsion characterised by the use of explosive or combustible propellant charges -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Propulsion is to be interpreted as the launching process of a missile within the barrel. Emphasis is made on the "characterised by", obviously only documents with characteristic propulsion systems are included in this subclass, since every single firearm launches missiles with a pyrotechnic propellant charge.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Projecting missiles without use of explosive or combustible propellant charge -> F41B - Launching rockets or torpedoes -> F41F3/00 - Separately loaded propellant charges -> F42B5/38 - Missile self-propulsion -> F42B15/00
[F41A 1/02] Hypervelocity missile propulsion using successive means for increasing the propulsive force, e.g. using successively initiated propellant charges arranged along the barrel length -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Multistage propulsion is generated by the firearm and NOT the missile, usually by using multiple sequentially triggered propellant chambers located at different locations along the barrel.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Multistage rocket-engine plants -> F02K9/76
[F41A 1/04] Missile propulsion using the combustion of a liquid, or gaseous fuel, e.g. hypergolic fuel -- F41A 1/04 relates to missile propulsion methods that produce thrust by combusting liquid or gaseous fuels, such as hypergolic propellants. The entry is classified under mechanical engineering, weapons, and functional features shared by small-arms and ordnance, focusing on the use of explosive or combustible charge systems for missile launch.
[F41A 1/06] Adjusting the range without varying elevation angle or propellant charge data, e.g. by venting a part of the propulsive charge gases, or by adjusting the capacity of the cartridge or combustion chamber -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Mechanical aspects of the range adjustments.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Adjusting the range by using gas-relieving ports in the barrel -> F41A21/28 - Gun mountings permitting traversing or elevating movement -> F41A27/00 - Elevating or traversing control systems for guns -> F41G5/00 - Arrangements on projectiles or missiles for range-reducing, range-increasing -> F42B10/00
[F41A 1/08] Recoilless guns, i.e. guns having propulsion means producing no recoil -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Guns with means for eliminating recoil by accelerating a mass in a direction opposite to the missile propulsion direction. Usually through Venturi pipes or by causing a part of the propellant gases to be expelled rearwardly to counteract the recoil forces.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Stocks specially adapted for recoil reduction -> F41C23/06 - Recoilless rocket launchers adapted to be carried and used by a person, e.g. bazookas -> F41F3/045
[F41A 1/10] a counter projectile being used to balance recoil -- **Definition:** This place covers:  The counter projectile can be a disintegrating projectile, e.g. comprising liquid or powder.
[F41A 11/00] Assembly or disassembly features -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Features and mechanisms and methods for assembling and dissasembling a firearm.  Modular weapon family concepts.  Articulation and collapsing of firearms for the PURPOSE of storage or transport.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Breakdown breech mechanisms -> F41A3/58 - Mounting of breech-blocks; Accessories for breech-blocks or breech-block mountings -> F41A3/64 - Triggers; Trigger mountings -> F41A19/10 - Modular firing mechanism units -> F41A19/15 - Barrel mounting means, e.g. releasable mountings for replaceable barrels -> F41A21/48 - Assembling or dismounting recoil elements or systems -> F41A25/26 - Folding or telescopic stocks or stock parts -> F41C23/04
[F41A 11/02] Modular concepts, e.g. weapon-family concepts -- F41A 11/02 covers modular weapon-family concepts, describing designs that use interchangeable or configurable components across related firearms or artillery. It addresses features that simplify assembly, disassembly and adaptation of parts, enabling a single platform to be reconfigured for different roles or calibres.
[F41A 11/04] Articulated or collapsible guns, i.e. with hinged or telescopic parts for transport or storage -- F41A 11/04 covers firearms that can be broken down, folded, or extended by hinges or telescoping sections to facilitate transport or storage. The classification applies to both small-arms and larger ordnance where the weapon's structure is articulated for compactness.
[F41A 11/06] Telescopic guns -- Telescopic guns are firearms whose barrel or other major components can be extended or retracted, allowing the weapon to change length for storage, transport, or concealment while maintaining functionality. The classification covers mechanisms, locking systems and methods that enable this telescoping action in small arms and related ordnance. It includes both the assembly features that allow telescoping and the disassembly procedures needed for maintenance or re-configuration.
[F41A 13/00] Cooling or heating systems -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Active systems specially adapted for cooling or heating firearm components, in particular the barrel and the firing chamber.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Barrels and gun tubes with fins or fins for cooling -> F41A21/02
[F41A 13/02] Heating systems -- F41A 13/02 covers heating systems used in weapons such as small arms, artillery and related ordnance. It includes devices that raise the temperature of barrels, chambers or ammunition to improve performance, prevent moisture or facilitate ignition, and may involve electric, chemical or fluid-based heating methods.
[F41A 13/04] Injecting fluids into barrels or cartridge chambers -- F41A 13/04 covers devices and methods for introducing liquids into the barrel or cartridge chamber of small-arms or ordnance, including systems that inject cooling, heating, propellant, or other fluids to control temperature, pressure, or performance during firing. It is a functional subset of weapon engineering that deals with fluid-injection mechanisms for barrels and chambers.
[F41A 13/06] Evacuating combustion gas from barrels -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Arrangements or systems for bleeding the gas from the barrel -> F41A5/26 - Blowers or turbines for evacuating or cooling guns, e.g. driven by combustion gas pressure or recoil -> F41A13/10
[F41A 13/08] Bore evacuators, i.e. chambers disposed around barrels for storing part of the combustion gas and subsequently injecting it into the barrel to provide suction -- F41A 13/08 covers bore evacuators, which are chambers located around a firearm's barrel that capture a portion of the combustion gases after a shot and then release them back into the barrel to create a suction effect, aiding in cooling, recoil reduction and clearing of residue.
[F41A 13/10] Blowers or turbines for evacuating or cooling guns, e.g. driven by combustion gas pressure or recoil -- F41A 13/10 designates blowers or turbines employed to evacuate gases or cool firearms, including cannons and small-arms, where the device is powered by the pressure of combustion gases, recoil energy or a similar source. It falls within the functional-features category for cooling or heating systems in weapons.
[F41A 13/12] Systems for cooling the outer surface of the barrel -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Blowers or turbines for evacuating or cooling guns, e.g. driven by combustion gas pressure or recoil -> F41A13/10 - Barrels or gun tubes with fins or ribs, e.g. for cooling -> F41A21/24
[F41A 15/00] Cartridge extractors, i.e. devices for pulling cartridges or cartridge cases at least partially out of the cartridge chamber -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Devices, mechanisms or tools for removing a spent cartridge case or a dud from the firing chamber or for unloading a cartridge from the chamber.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Blowers or turbines for evacuating or cooling guns, e.g. driven by combustion gas pressure or recoil -> F41A9/54 - Barrels or gun tubes with fins or ribs, e.g. for cooling -> F41C9/08 - Means for removing duds or misfires in rocket throwers -> F41F3/058  **Glossary:** - Dud:: Cartridge, or separately loaded projectile or case, remaining stuck in the firing chamber after a misfire - Spent cartridge case:: case remaining after the projectile was launched
[F41A 15/02] for revolver-type guns, e.g. revolvers -- F41A 15/02 covers cartridge extractor mechanisms designed for revolver-type firearms, enabling the partial or complete removal of spent cartridges from the chamber. The classification falls under functional features common to small arms and ordnance within mechanical engineering, specifically weapons.
[F41A 15/04] specially adapted for cartridge cases being deformed when fired, e.g. of plastics -- F41A 15/04 covers cartridge extractors that are specially adapted to handle cartridge cases that become deformed during firing, such as those made of plastic. These devices pull partially ejected cases out of the chamber, ensuring reliable extraction for weapons using brittle or flexible ammunition.
[F41A 15/06] for breakdown guns -- F41A 15/06 covers cartridge extractors designed for breakdown guns, devices that pull spent cartridges or cases partially out of the chamber to facilitate cleaning, maintenance or disassembly of small-arms and ordnance. The classification applies to mechanisms that engage the cartridge rim or case base to withdraw it without full ejection, typically used in guns built for rapid breakdown or inspection.
[F41A 15/08] for block-action guns -- F41A 15/08 covers block-action firearms, where a solid block moves to lock the breech and to open it for loading and unloading. The code pertains to the cartridge-extractor mechanisms used in such guns, enabling partial or complete removal of spent cases from the chamber. It falls under functional features common to both small arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 15/10] of sliding-block type -- The F41A 15/10 subclass refers to sliding-block cartridge extractors, mechanisms that pull a cartridge or its case at least partially out of the chamber in small-arms or artillery. The device uses a block that slides transversely to engage the rim or case head and extract it for ejection or manual removal.
[F41A 15/12] for bolt-action guns -- F41A 15/12 designates cartridge extractor mechanisms that are adapted for bolt-action firearms. It falls under the mechanical-engineering weapons subclass dealing with functional features common to small arms and ordnance, specifically devices that pull spent cartridges partially out of the chamber.
[F41A 15/14] the ejector being mounted on or within the bolt;
[F41A 15/16] the ejector being mounted on the breech housing or frame -- F41A 15/16 covers cartridge extractors that are integrated into the breech housing or frame of firearms and artillery, describing the ejector's mounting arrangement. The classification applies to mechanisms that pull spent cartridges or cases partially out of the chamber in both small arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 15/18] for guns with forwardly slidable barrels -- The CPC subclass F41A 15/18 covers firearms whose barrels can slide forward, a configuration used to aid loading or extraction. It falls under functional features common to small-arms and ordnance, specifically mechanisms that assist in pulling cartridges or cases from the chamber. This subclass is part of the broader hierarchy of mechanical engineering weapons.
[F41A 15/20] specially adapted for caseless-ammunition duds -- F41A 15/20 covers cartridge-extractor mechanisms that are specially adapted to handle duds of caseless ammunition. The classification falls within mechanical-engineering weapons features dealing with devices for pulling cartridges or cases partly out of the chamber.
[F41A 15/22] Tools for extracting cartridges -- F41A 15/22 covers tools that pull cartridges or spent cases from the chamber of firearms, including manual or powered extractors for small-arms and larger ordnance. These devices enable safe removal of ammunition for reloading, inspection or maintenance.
[F41A 17/00] Safety arrangements, e.g. safeties -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Devices and mechanism covering all aspects of firearm safety, including:  preventing unauthorised use  blocking or rendering ineffective diverse functional parts of the firearm to prevent inadvertent firing  inhibiting firing in a particular direction  preventing cook off or hang fire  preventing release of magazine  last round safeties
[F41A 17/02] Key-operated safeties -- F41A 17/02 covers key-operated safety devices for firearms and artillery, wherein a key is used to engage or release the safety mechanism to prevent accidental discharge.
[F41A 17/04] Safeties of the combination-lock type -- F41A 17/04 covers combination-lock type safeties used on firearms and artillery. The class includes mechanisms that block trigger or firing actions until a correct numeric or rotating combination is entered, providing a secure, reset-able safety arrangement that can be set and released by the operator.
[F41A 17/06] Electric or electromechanical safeties -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Includes electric or electromagnetic means for preventing or enabling the use of a firearm. Also includes the remote control or a firearm, in the sense that a central control instance can block or release the use of the firearm. Also includes remote monitoring of firearm movement and/or usage. Further includes passive, post-incident safety means such as a black box data recording.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Safeties of the combination-lock type -> F41A17/04 - Safeties for inhibiting firing in a specified direction, e.g. at a friendly person or at a protected area -> F41A17/08 - Remote triggering of a firearm -> F41A19/08 - Electric firing mechanisms -> F41A19/56
[F41A 17/063] comprising a transponder -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Includes means for remote weapon control and monitoring.
[F41A 17/066] having means for recognizing biometric parameters, e.g. voice control, finger print or palm print control -- This subclass covers safety mechanisms for small arms or ordnance that incorporate biometric identification, such as voice, fingerprint or palm-print recognition, to enable or restrict firing. It specifies the use of personal-characteristic detection as a control feature within weapon safety arrangements.
[F41A 17/08] for inhibiting firing in a specified direction, e.g. at a friendly person or at a protected area -- F41A 17/08 covers safety features that restrict a weapon's discharge to a predetermined direction, preventing firing toward friendly personnel or protected zones. Such arrangements may include directional safeties, muzzle-lock devices or other mechanisms that inhibit firing when the weapon is aimed outside an authorized sector. This subclass falls under functional details common to small arms and ordnance.
[F41A 17/10] Firing mechanisms with elevation stop -- F41A 17/10 covers firing mechanisms that incorporate an elevation-stop feature, which limits the upward travel of a weapon's barrel or breech during operation. The classification is used for safety-related components that prevent over-elevation in small arms and larger ordnance, ensuring controlled firing angles and reducing the risk of accidental discharge. It falls under functional features common to both smallarms and cannons within the mechanical-engineering weapons hierarchy.
[F41A 17/12] Firing mechanisms with anti-canting safety -- F41A 17/12 covers firing mechanisms that include anti-canting safety features, designed to prevent a weapon from discharging unless it is correctly aligned with the target axis. The classification applies to both small arms and larger ordnance where such safety arrangements are integrated into the trigger or firing system.
[F41A 17/14] Double-loading prevention -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Features to avoid jamming in the cartridge chamber caused by an attempt to load a second cartridge in an already loaded chamber.
[F41A 17/16] Cook-off prevention, i.e. prevention of spontaneous firing of a cartridge by chamber wall heat -- The CPC subclass F41A 17/16 covers safety arrangements that prevent a cartridge from igniting spontaneously because of heat transferred from the chamber wall, often called "cook-off" prevention. It includes measures such as thermal insulation, cooling systems, heat-absorbing materials and other designs that keep the propellant temperature below ignition thresholds.
[F41A 17/18] Hang-fire prevention -- **Glossary:** - Hang fire: an unexpected delay between the triggering of a firearm and the ignition of the propellant
[F41A 17/20] Grip or stock safeties, i.e. safeties disengaged by clasping the grip or stock -- F41A 17/20 covers safety mechanisms for firearms that are released when the shooter grasps the grip or stock, allowing the weapon to fire only when held properly. These grip- or stock-activated safeties are used to prevent accidental discharge in small arms and ordnance.
[F41A 17/22] acting on the trigger -- It covers mechanisms that operate a firearm trigger, including manual, automatic or assisted actions that release the firing mechanism. The class also includes safety devices that intervene in trigger actuation and methods for resetting or decocking.
[F41A 17/24] acting on the firing pin -- F41A 17/24 covers mechanisms that operate the firing pin in small-arms or ordnance, including triggers, hammers, or safety devices that move the pin to initiate a discharge, as well as related control or release systems.
[F41A 17/26] acting on the hammer -- The F41A 17/26 entry covers mechanisms that act on the hammer of firearms, including safety devices such as hammer blocks, manual safeties and other features that intervene with hammer movement. It pertains to functional details shared by small-arms and larger ordnance within the mechanical-engineering weapons category.
[F41A 17/28] acting on the sear -- The F41A 17/28 subclass covers mechanisms that act on a firearm's sear, including devices that engage, disengage, or hold the sear to control trigger movement and fire safety. It pertains to functional features shared by small-arms and larger ordnance, such as safeties and trigger-reset elements that influence the firing sequence.
[F41A 17/30] Multiple safeties, i.e. acting on at least one element of the firing mechanism and at least one other element of the gun, e.g. the moving barrel
[F41A 17/32] the other element being the breech-block or bolt -- This CPC subgroup deals with safety devices for small-arms and ordnance where the cooperating part is the breech-block or bolt, covering mechanisms that lock, release or otherwise protect the breech during firing.
[F41A 17/34] Magazine safeties -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Safety plugs, e.g. for plugging-up cartridge chambers -> F41A17/44
[F41A 17/36] locking the gun in a safety condition when the magazine is empty or removed -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Last round safety for magazine fed smallarm, the follower of the magazine activating or acting on a gun-locking element such as a bolt or pistol slide. Means for preventing a loaded cartridge to be fired when the magazine is removed from the smallarm.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Safety plugs, e.g. for plugging-up cartridge chambers -> F41A17/44
[F41A 17/38] locking the magazine in the gun -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Safety plugs, e.g. for plugging-up cartridge chambers -> F41A17/44
[F41A 17/40] Last-round safeties -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Means for setting an automatic, non-magazine-fed and usually medium or large calibre gun's feeding, ramming, loading and/or firing mechanism into a safe/inactive configuration when the last cartridge is chambered or fired, e.g. in Gatling type guns.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Locking a smallarm in a safety condition when the magazine is empty or removed -> F41A17/34
[F41A 17/42] Safeties for locking the breech-block or bolt in a safety position -- F41A 17/42 pertains to safety mechanisms that lock the breech-block or bolt of a firearm in a safe position, preventing unintended discharge. It includes designs where the breech is held closed or immobilized by a safety device when the weapon is not being fired.
[F41A 17/44] Safety plugs, e.g. for plugging-up cartridge chambers -- This CPC subclass covers safety plugs used to seal cartridge chambers in firearms and artillery, providing a protective barrier that prevents accidental discharge or ignition of the propellant.
[F41A 17/46] Trigger safeties, i.e. means for preventing trigger movement -- The subclass covers safety mechanisms that block or restrict trigger movement to prevent accidental discharge of firearms, including manual or automatic devices that engage the trigger mechanism. It applies to both small-arms and larger ordnance where such trigger-safety arrangements are employed.
[F41A 17/48] Automatically operated trigger safeties, i.e. operated by breech opening or closing movement -- F41A 17/48 covers trigger safety mechanisms that engage or release automatically when the breech is opened or closed, without manual actuation. These safeties are used in firearms and artillery to prevent accidental discharge during loading or unloading. The classification falls under mechanical engineering, weapons, and functional features common to smallarms and ordnance.
[F41A 17/50] by breakdown action -- The F41A 17/50 subclass pertains to safety arrangements for firearms that function by a breakdown action, where the weapon is rendered inoperable through intentional separation or disassembly of components when an unsafe condition occurs. It includes mechanisms that break or lock parts of the gun to prevent accidental or unauthorized firing.
[F41A 17/52] Thumb-operated sliding safeties mounted on the upside of the stock, e.g. for shotguns -- It covers thumb-operated sliding safety mechanisms mounted on the upper side of a firearm's stock, typically on shotguns, allowing the shooter to engage or disengage the safety with the thumb.
[F41A 17/54] Protecting-caps for trigger guards -- Protecting caps for trigger guards are safety covers, either removable or fixed, that shield a firearm's trigger mechanism from accidental contact, debris or moisture, thereby improving user safety and weapon reliability.
[F41A 17/56] Sear safeties, i.e. means for rendering ineffective an intermediate lever transmitting trigger movement to firing pin, hammer, bolt or sear -- The subclass deals with safety devices that interrupt the action of an intermediate lever - the sear - so that trigger movement cannot drive the firing pin, hammer, bolt or similar components. Such mechanisms physically block or disable the lever to prevent accidental discharge and are applied to both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 17/58] automatically operated, i.e. operated by breech opening or closing movement -- This classification covers safety mechanisms for firearms and artillery that are automatically engaged by the motion of the breech when it opens or closes. It includes devices that trigger safeties without manual actuation, typical for small arms and larger ordnance. The CPC code is F41A 17/58.
[F41A 17/60] by breakdown action -- F41A 17/60 covers safety mechanisms for small-arms and ordnance that function by a breakdown action, meaning the device is rendered safe by being taken apart or separated into components. It applies to designs where disengaging parts, such as bolts or casings, prevents accidental discharge.
[F41A 17/62] Thumb-operated sliding safeties mounted on the upside of the stock, e.g. for shotguns -- The entry covers thumb-operated sliding safety mechanisms that are mounted on the upper side of the stock, typically found on shotguns and similar firearms. These safeties provide a quick manual method to block the trigger or firing mechanism, enhancing user safety during handling and transport.
[F41A 17/64] Firing-pin safeties, i.e. means for preventing movement of slidably- mounted strikers -- F41A 17/64 covers safety devices that prevent a firing-pin or similarly sliding striker from moving forward unintentionally, thereby averting accidental discharge of small arms or ordnance. The classification includes blocking levers, springs, latches or other mechanisms that lock or restrict striker travel until a deliberate action releases them.
[F41A 17/66] automatically operated, i.e. operated by breech opening or closing movement -- This group covers safety devices that are triggered automatically by the movement of a weapon's breech, such as mechanisms that engage when the breech is opened or closed to prevent unintended firing. The features are integral to small arms and artillery safety systems.
[F41A 17/68] by breakdown action -- F41A 17/68 covers safety mechanisms for firearms or artillery that render the weapon inoperative by a breakdown action, such as a component that fragments, disassembles, or otherwise fails when a hazardous condition occurs, preventing further firing.
[F41A 17/70] Thumb-operated sliding safeties mounted on the upside of the stock, e.g. for shotguns -- This subclass covers firearms safety devices that are operated by a thumb and slide along the top of the stock, such as the safety lever found on many shotguns. The mechanism is mounted on the upper rear of the stock and provides a manual means to engage or disengage the weapon's firing capability.
[F41A 17/72] trigger-operated, i.e. the movement of the trigger bringing a firing-pin safety into inoperative position during the firing -- The F41A 17/72 class covers firearms where pulling the trigger mechanically shifts a firing-pin safety into an inoperative position as the weapon fires, ensuring the safety is disengaged only during the firing stroke. This applies to trigger-operated safety mechanisms in small arms and related ordnance.
[F41A 17/74] Hammer safeties, i.e. means for preventing the hammer from hitting the cartridge or the firing pin -- F41A 17/74 covers hammer-safety mechanisms for firearms, devices that prevent the hammer from striking the cartridge or firing pin unless the weapon is deliberately fired. These safety features are used in small-arms and larger ordnance to reduce the risk of accidental discharge. The classification groups any structural or functional elements designed to block hammer impact.
[F41A 17/76] automatically operated, i.e. operated by breech opening or closing movement -- F41A 17/76 covers firearms or ordnance that are automatically cycled by the motion of the breech when it opens or closes, providing self-loading or self-ejecting functions without manual actuation. The feature is used as a safety or functional element common to small arms and larger weapons.
[F41A 17/78] by breakdown action -- F41A 17/78 designates safety arrangements for firearms and artillery that function through a breakdown action, meaning the device automatically disables the weapon when a critical component fails or is damaged. This subclass falls under functional features common to small-arms and ordnance within the mechanical engineering weapons field.
[F41A 17/80] Thumb-operated sliding safeties mounted on the upside of the stock, e.g. for shotguns -- F41A 17/80 covers thumb-operated sliding safety mechanisms mounted on the upper side of a firearm's stock, such as those used on shotguns. The classification pertains to safety arrangements common to small arms and ordnance within the mechanical engineering weapons hierarchy.
[F41A 17/82] trigger-operated, i.e. the movement of the trigger bringing a hammer safety into inoperative position during firing -- F41A 17/82 covers safety mechanisms in firearms where pulling the trigger simultaneously disengages a hammer safety, allowing the weapon to fire. The classification applies to designs in which the trigger movement directly renders the safety inoperative, a feature common to small arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 19/00] Firing or trigger mechanisms -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Mechanisms or systems for firing, i.e. initiating the ignition of the propellant charge by mechanical, fluid-operated, pyrotechnic, optical or electric means.  Cocking mechanisms, i.e. for bringing mechanical firing means in a pre-tensioned, ready to fire state (cocked).  Further includes means for controlling number of shots fired, firing rate and synchronisation.  **Glossary:** - Sear:: the part of the trigger mechanism which holds the hammer or striker back until the correct amount of pressure has been applied to the trigger; at which point the hammer or striker is released to discharge the weapon. The sear may be a separate part or can be a surface incorporated into the trigger - DA: Double action trigger performs the action of cocking during the initial trigger pull movement and releases the hammer or striker at the pressure point - DA/SA: Double action / Single action Double action for first shot and single action for subsequent shots - DAO: Double action onlyDouble action for all shots - SA: Single actiontrigger only performs the action of releasing the hammer or striker - Cocking: bringing mechanical firing means in a pre-tensioned, ready to fire state (cocked)
[F41A 19/01] Counting means indicating the number of shots fired -- A counting means that displays the number of projectiles discharged is classified under F41A 19/01. It pertains to mechanical or electronic shot-counter devices incorporated in trigger or firing systems of small arms and larger ordnance, providing an indication of shots fired for monitoring or safety purposes.
[F41A 19/02] Burst limiters -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Means for allowing a predefined number of of rounds to be fired upon a single trigger action.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Arrangements for the selection of automatic and semi-automatic fire in bolt action guns having a sliding striker -> F41A19/33 - Arrangements for the selection of automatic and semi-automatic fire in bolt action guns having a hammer -> F41A19/46 - Burst limiters -> F41A19/67
[F41A 19/03] Shot-velocity control -- F41A 19/03 pertains to mechanisms that regulate the velocity of a projectile as it leaves the barrel, covering devices such as variable-pressure chambers, adjustable propellant charges, and electronic or mechanical systems that modulate shot speed in firearms and artillery. It is used to classify inventions that actively control muzzle velocity for safety, accuracy, or tactical purposes.
[F41A 19/04] by controlling the time of release of the firing pin or hammer -- The subclass deals with mechanisms that control when the firing pin or hammer is released, providing timed or delayed ignition for small-arms and ordnance. It covers devices that adjust the release interval to modify the firing sequence.
[F41A 19/05] Synchronising for firing through the propeller of an aircraft -- F41A 19/05 covers mechanisms that synchronize a firearm's firing with a rotating aircraft propeller, ensuring that projectiles pass safely between the blades. It addresses the timing and control systems required for firing through the propeller arc, a key feature of early fighter aircraft armament.
[F41A 19/06] Mechanical firing mechanisms, -- F41A 19/06 covers mechanical firing mechanisms used in firearms and artillery, including trigger assemblies, hammer systems, and related components that initiate projectile discharge. It is classified under weapons functional features and pertains to the design and operation of trigger or firing devices in small arms and ordnance.
[F41A 19/07] press-button actuated, e.g. with thumb rest -- This subclass covers firearms whose firing or trigger mechanism is operated by a press-button, typically positioned for thumb activation and often incorporating a thumb rest. It applies to small arms and ordnance where the usual lever-type trigger is replaced by a button-type actuator.
[F41A 19/08] remote actuated -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Remote safing and monitoring of firearms -> F41A17/06
[F41A 19/09] Auxiliary trigger devices -- F41A 19/09 covers auxiliary trigger devices for weapons, including small-arms and ordnance, that supplement or replace the main firing mechanism. It includes secondary triggers, safety-release triggers, decocker triggers and other add-on mechanisms that control or modify the act of firing. This subclass falls under functional features common to firearms and cannons.
[F41A 19/10] Triggers -- F41A 19/10 covers the mechanisms that initiate the discharge of firearms and artillery, including pulls, levers and electronic triggers that release the firing pin or primer. It pertains to design features that control timing and safety of shot initiation in small arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 19/11] Trigger guards -- F41A 19/11 designates trigger guards, components that surround or shield the trigger of small arms and artillery to prevent accidental discharge and protect the user. The classification covers guards made of metal, polymer or other materials, including integrated or detachable designs, and related mechanisms that interact with the trigger assembly.
[F41A 19/12] Sears -- F41A 19/12 pertains to the sear, the component of a firearm's trigger mechanism that holds the hammer or striker in the cocked position and releases it when the trigger is pulled. It classifies designs, constructions and variations of sears used in small-arms and artillery firing systems.
[F41A 19/13] Percussion or firing pins, i.e. fixed or slidably-mounted striker elements -- This CPC subclass covers percussion or firing pins used in firearms and artillery, including both fixed pins and those that slide to strike a primer. It pertains to the firing or trigger mechanisms of small arms and ordnance within the mechanical engineering weapons field.
[F41A 19/14] Hammers, i.e. pivotably-mounted striker elements -- F41A 19/14 covers hammer components that are mounted on a pivot and act as striker elements within firing or trigger mechanisms of small-arms and ordnance such as cannons. The classification applies to designs where the hammer swings to impact a primer or firing pin, enabling the weapon to discharge.
[F41A 19/15] Modular firing mechanism units -- F41A 19/15 covers modular firing mechanism units used in small arms and larger ordnance. The classification pertains to interchangeable or sub-assembly components that provide trigger, hammer, or firing-pin functions and can be combined to form complete firing systems.
[F41A 19/16] Adjustable firing mechanisms -- F41A 19/16 relates to adjustable firing mechanisms, covering devices that allow the trigger pull weight, sear position or timing to be varied in small arms and artillery. The classification includes mechanisms such as adjustable triggers, variable sear springs and modular trigger groups that enable users to fine-tune firing characteristics for different operational requirements.
[F41A 19/17] Hair-trigger mechanisms -- **Definition:** This place covers:  A trigger allowing a shooter to have a greatly reduced trigger pull (the resistance of the trigger) while maintaining a degree of safety in the field
[F41A 19/18] for multibarrel guns -- The F41A 19/18 subclass covers the firing or trigger mechanisms specifically designed for multibarrel guns, detailing the arrangements that enable simultaneous or sequential discharge of several barrels. It includes the linkage, sear, and control systems that coordinate multiple firing actions within a single weapon system.
[F41A 19/183] for multiple guns, i.e. a plurality of guns being actuated by a single firing mechanism -- F41A 19/183 relates to firing or trigger mechanisms that operate a plurality of guns from a single control, such as multi-barrel firearms or weapon systems where several barrels are fired together or in sequence by one trigger.
[F41A 19/186] having only one striker element and more than one trigger, each trigger acting on a single element  firing mechanisms having only one trigger and only one striker element  F41A19/22
[F41A 19/19] with single-trigger firing possibility -- **Definition:** This place covers:  One trigger can cause the firing of more than one barrel
[F41A 19/20] Double-trigger arrangements having the possibility of single-trigger actuation -- **Definition:** This place covers:  One trigger per barrel is present, but the shooter has the choice of firing each barrel with its dedicated trigger, or both at the same time by actuation of a single trigger (after appropriate setting).
[F41A 19/21] having only one trigger -- The F41A 19/21 subclass designates weapons whose firing system uses a single trigger to initiate discharge, encompassing both small-arms and larger ordnance where no additional or separate triggers are provided. It applies to mechanisms in which all firing functions are controlled by that one trigger.
[F41A 19/22] and only one striker element -- F41A 19/22 covers firing or trigger mechanisms for small-arms and ordnance that employ a single striker element to initiate discharge. The class is used for designs where one striker alone performs the impact or ignition function in weapons such as pistols, rifles or cannons. It falls under the broader mechanical-engineering category of functional features common to both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 19/23] rotatable about an axis parallel to the barrel axis for firing subsequent barrels -- F41A 19/23 covers weapons whose multiple barrels rotate around an axis parallel to the barrel line to enable successive firing, such as rotary cannons and multi-barrel machine guns. The classification addresses the mechanism that indexes each barrel into the firing position after each discharge.
[F41A 19/24] Release-trigger mechanisms, i.e. the striker element being released during the return movement of the trigger subsequent to trigger pull -- F41A 19/24 covers release-trigger mechanisms in firearms where the striker element is held back during the trigger pull and released as the trigger returns to its forward position. This pertains to functional features common to small arms and ordnance.
[F41A 19/25] having only slidably-mounted striker elements, i.e. percussion or firing pins -- This subclass covers weapons whose firing mechanism relies only on striker elements that move linearly, such as percussion or firing pins, without any rotating or camming parts. It applies to small arms and ordnance where the sliding striker impacts the primer to initiate discharge.
[F41A 19/26] the percussion or firing pin and the breech-block or bolt forming one piece -- F41A 19/26 covers a combined percussion or firing pin and breech-block/bolt formed as a single component, used in firearms to initiate discharge and seal the chamber. This integration simplifies the trigger mechanism and is relevant to small-arms and artillery where a unified firing element improves reliability and reduces moving parts.
[F41A 19/27] the percussion or firing pin being movable relative to the breech-block -- F41A 19/27 covers designs in which the percussion or firing pin can move independently of the breech-block, allowing it to strike the primer while the breech remains stationary, a functional feature used in small-arms and larger ordnance to improve reliability or safety.
[F41A 19/28] propelled by a cam or lever when the breech-block or bolt arrives in a closing position -- F41A 19/28 covers firing or trigger mechanisms in small arms or ordnance that are driven by a cam or lever which activates when the breech-block or bolt reaches its closing position, providing a timed propulsion of the firing element. This subclass falls under mechanical engineering, weapons, functional features common to both smallarms and ordnance.
[F41A 19/29] propelled by a spring under tension -- F41A 19/29 covers weapon firing or trigger mechanisms that are driven by a spring held under tension, such as the spring-loaded firing pins or hammers used in small arms and artillery. The subclass details the design and operation of these spring-propelled components within the broader category of functional features common to both smallarms and ordnance.
[F41A 19/30] in bolt-action guns -- F41A 19/30 relates to the firing and trigger mechanisms of bolt-action guns, covering the bolt, firing pin and associated parts that load, lock and discharge cartridges in rifles and similar weapons. It defines functional features common to both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 19/31] Sear arrangements therefor -- F41A 19/31 covers the design and arrangement of sears within firing or trigger mechanisms of small arms and artillery. It includes the positioning, linking and interaction of sears that control hammer or bolt release during discharge. The classification is used for patents describing specific sear configurations in weapons.
[F41A 19/32] for catching the percussion or firing pin after each shot, i.e. in single-shot or semi-automatic firing mode -- F41A 19/32 covers devices that capture the percussion or firing pin after each discharge, ensuring the pin is retained and ready for the next shot in single-shot or semi-automatic firearms. It relates to functional features common to small arms and ordnance.
[F41A 19/33] Arrangements for the selection of automatic or semi-automatic fire -- F41A 19/33 covers arrangements that let a shooter choose between automatic and semi-automatic fire in weapons. It pertains to fire-selection mechanisms integrated in the trigger or firing system of small arms and artillery, enabling mode switching for controlled or continuous discharge.
[F41A 19/34] Cocking mechanisms -- F41A 19/34 covers cocking mechanisms used in firearms and artillery, which draw the firing or trigger component back to a ready position and retain it until release. The class includes hand-operated, lever, lever-spring, or other devices that store energy for a subsequent discharge, applicable to both small arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 19/35] Double-action mechanisms, i.e. the cocking being effected during the first part of the trigger pull movement -- F41A 19/35 covers double-action firing mechanisms in weapons, where pulling the trigger both cocks and releases the hammer or striker in a single motion. The design eliminates a separate manual cocking step, improving speed and safety.
[F41A 19/36] in block-action guns -- In block-action guns the firing mechanism uses a single solid block that moves to open, lock and close the breech, providing a combined extraction, ejection and sealing action. This design is applied to certain firearms and artillery where the block slides or rotates to chamber and fire a round.
[F41A 19/37] Cocking mechanisms -- F41A 19/37 covers cocking mechanisms used to prepare a firearm for firing. It includes springs, levers and other devices that draw the hammer, striker or bolt back in small arms and artillery. The subclass falls under functional features of weapons in the CPC hierarchy.
[F41A 19/38] Double-action mechanisms, i.e. the cocking being effected during the first part of the trigger pull movement -- F41A 19/38 pertains to double-action firing mechanisms in weapons where the cocking of the lock is carried out during the first portion of the trigger pull, combining cocking and release in a single motion. It falls under functional features common to small arms and ordnance within the mechanical engineering hierarchy.
[F41A 19/39] Cocking mechanisms for other types of guns, e.g. fixed breech-block types, forwardly-slidable barrel types
[F41A 19/40] Double-action mechanisms, i.e. the cocking being effected during the first part of the trigger pull movement -- F41A 19/40 covers double-action firing mechanisms where pulling the trigger both cocks and releases the hammer or striker, so the cocking occurs in the initial portion of the trigger movement. [F41A 19/41] for breakdown guns -- The subclass F41A 19/41 covers firing-or-trigger mechanisms specifically designed for breakdown guns, which are firearms that can be separated into component parts for easy transport, assembly, or maintenance. It includes technical features of triggers, hammers, sears and related elements that enable rapid dismantling and re-assembly while preserving reliable operation.
[F41A 19/42] having at least one hammer -- The subclass F41A 19/42 covers weapons whose firing or trigger mechanism includes at least one hammer that strikes the firing pin or primer. It applies to small-arms, artillery and other ordnance where a hammer is a functional component of the ignition system.
[F41A 19/43] in bolt-action guns -- F41A 19/43 covers the bolt-action mechanism of firearms, detailing the design and operation of bolts that load, lock, and fire a cartridge in rifles and other small arms. It includes features such as the bolt handle, rearward movement, locking lugs and camming surfaces that control firing and extraction.
[F41A 19/44] Sear arrangements therefor -- F41A 19/44 relates to the design and arrangement of sears, the mechanisms that retain and release the hammer or firing pin in firearms and artillery. It covers various configurations, linkages and combined sear groups that control the initiation of discharge in both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 19/45] for catching the hammer after each shot, i.e. in single-shot or semi-automatic firing mode -- The CPC entry F41A 19/45 refers to mechanisms that catch or retain the hammer after each discharge, used in single-shot and semi-automatic firearms. It falls under firing or trigger mechanisms within the functional features common to smallarms and ordnance, a subcategory of weapons in mechanical engineering.
[F41A 19/46] Arrangements for the selection of automatic or semi-automatic fire -- The F41A 19/46 subclass covers mechanical arrangements that enable the shooter to choose between fully automatic and semi-automatic modes of fire in small-arms and artillery weapons, detailing trigger or fire-control systems that switch firing rates. It is part of the broader functional features of weapons within mechanical engineering.
[F41A 19/47] Cocking mechanisms -- F41A 19/47 designates the cocking mechanisms used to prepare a firearm's firing system for discharge. It includes devices that draw back a hammer, striker or firing pin and hold it under tension until released by the trigger, covering both small-arms and larger ordnance. The subclass is part of the functional features common to weapons within the mechanical engineering hierarchy.
[F41A 19/48] Double-action mechanisms, i.e. the cocking being effected during the first part of the trigger pull movement -- F41A 19/48 is a CPC subclass within mechanical-engineering weapons, covering functional features of small-arms and ordnance firing mechanisms. It specifically pertains to double-action systems where the cocking of the hammer or striker is performed during the first part of the trigger pull.
[F41A 19/49] in block-action guns -- F41A 19/49 covers block-action guns, a type of firearm where a solid block moves to open and close the breech for loading and firing. It is a subcategory of firing or trigger mechanisms within functional features common to both small arms and ordnance. This entry groups inventions that use a block to seal the chamber and release it during the discharge cycle.
[F41A 19/50] Cocking mechanisms -- F41A 19/50 covers cocking mechanisms used in small arms and larger ordnance to draw back or tension the firing or trigger system before discharge. The code includes devices that manually or automatically reset the firing mechanism, such as cocking levers, springs, and loading handles. It is classified under functional features of weapons in the CPC hierarchy.
[F41A 19/51] Double-action mechanisms, i.e. the cocking being effected during the first part of the trigger pull movement -- The subclass F41A 19/51 classifies double-action trigger mechanisms in firearms, where the hammer or striker is automatically cocked during the first part of the trigger pull before the firing impulse is released. It applies to both small arms and larger ordnance that use this integrated cock-and-release system.
[F41A 19/52] Cocking mechanisms for other types of guns, e.g. fixed breech-block types, revolvers -- The CPC entry F41A 19/52 covers mechanisms that draw back or pre-load the firing action in guns other than typical rifles, such as fixed breech-block firearms and revolvers. It includes designs for cocking levers, rods, springs and related components that ready the weapon for discharge.
[F41A 19/53] Double-action mechanisms, i.e. the cocking being effected during the first part of the trigger pull movement -- F41A 19/53 covers double-action firing mechanisms in firearms, where pulling the trigger both cocks and releases the hammer or striker in a single motion. This design eliminates the need for a separate manual cocking step, enabling faster firing sequences.
[F41A 19/54] for breakdown guns -- This subclass covers firearms where the barrel or breech is opened by a hinged or pivoting action to load and unload cartridges, commonly called break-action or breakdown guns. It includes the design of the trigger and firing mechanism specific to such opening methods.
[F41A 19/55] Fluid-operated firing mechanisms -- F41A 19/55 covers firing mechanisms for weapons that are powered by fluid pressure, such as hydraulic or pneumatic systems that move the trigger, firing pin or bolt to initiate discharge. The classification applies to both small-arms and larger ordnance where fluid actuation replaces mechanical springs or manual pull.
[F41A 19/56] Ignition of the propellant charge by contact with air heated by adiabatic compression -- F41A 19/56 covers methods where the propellant in a gun or small-arm is ignited by air that becomes hot enough through adiabatic compression to ignite the charge on contact. This includes designs that rely on rapid compression of gas in the barrel or chamber to provide the necessary heat without an external primer.
[F41A 19/57] Firing mechanisms operating with primer cartridge -- The subclass F41A 19/57 covers firing mechanisms that operate with a primer cartridge, such as hammers, strikers or firing pins that strike a self-contained primer to ignite the propellant. It pertains to small-arms and ordnance where the primer is packaged within a cartridge case, describing the functional details of trigger-actuated ignition systems.
[F41A 19/58] Electric firing mechanisms -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Means for electrically releasing mechanical firing means or for firing electrical primers in cartridges. Further includes mechanisms where the primer charge is ignited optically, e.g. by a laser or intense light.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Firing mechanisms with elevation stop -> F41A17/10 - Firing mechanisms with anti-canting safety -> F41A17/12
[F41A 19/59] Electromechanical firing mechanisms, i.e. the mechanical striker element being propelled or released by electric means -- The CPC subclass F41A 19/59 covers electromechanical firing mechanisms in weapons where the striker or firing pin is moved or released by an electric actuation, such as solenoid-driven or motor-powered devices. It applies to both small arms and larger ordnance where an electric signal initiates the mechanical impact that ignites the propellant.
[F41A 19/60] characterised by the means for generating electric energy -- F41A 19/60 relates to weapon firing or trigger mechanisms that incorporate a means for generating electric energy, such as internal batteries, generators, or inductive coils, to power the operation of the trigger or ignition system. It covers firearms and ordnance where the electrical source is produced by the weapon itself rather than supplied externally.
[F41A 19/61] Inductive generators -- F41A 19/61 covers inductive generators that produce electrical energy for use in firing or trigger mechanisms of small arms and larger ordnance, such as cannons. The devices typically convert mechanical motion during the firing sequence into pulse power for ignition or electronic control.
[F41A 19/62] Piezoelectric generators -- F41A 19/62 covers piezoelectric generators used in firing or trigger mechanisms of weapons, including small arms and ordnance. It includes devices that convert mechanical impact or pressure into electrical energy to power or assist triggers, impact sensors, or self-charging firing systems.
[F41A 19/63] having means for contactless transmission of electric energy, e.g. by induction, by sparking gap -- F41A 19/63 covers firing or trigger mechanisms for weapons that incorporate contactless electric-energy transmission, such as inductive coupling or a sparking gap, to supply power to the trigger or related components without direct electrical contacts.
[F41A 19/64] for automatic or burst-firing mode -- The code covers mechanisms that enable automatic or burst fire in firearms and artillery, including components that control multiple successive discharges per trigger actuation. It pertains to trigger and firing system designs that produce a continuous or rapid series of shots without manual re-cocking.
[F41A 19/65] for giving ripple fire, i.e. using electric sequencer switches for timed multiple-charge launching, e.g. for rocket launchers -- F41A 19/65 covers devices that provide ripple fire, meaning a sequence of launches triggered by electric sequencer switches that time multiple charges for successive firing. The classification applies to systems such as rocket launchers that execute timed multiple-charge discharges.
[F41A 19/66] Electronic shot-velocity control -- F41A 19/66 covers electronic means for regulating the velocity of a projectile as it leaves the barrel of small-arms or larger ordnance. It includes sensor-based feedback, digital control units and power-modulated firing circuits that adjust propellant ignition or breech pressure to achieve a predetermined shot speed.

[F41A 19/69] Electric contacts or switches peculiar thereto -- The classification F41A 19/69 in the CPC covers electric contacts or switches that are specifically used in firing or trigger mechanisms of small-arms and ordnance. It includes electrical initiators, contactors, igniters and electronic trigger devices that control the discharge of a weapon.
[F41A 19/70] Electric firing pins -- The CPC code F41A 19/70 covers electrically powered firing pins used in small-arms and larger ordnance. It refers to the component that, when energized, strikes a primer to initiate the propellant charge, forming part of the electronic trigger or ignition system.
[F41A 21/00] Barrels -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Includes barrels, tubes; attachments and mounting means therefore. The cartridge chamber or firing chamber is an integral part of the barrel, except for, e.g. revolver type guns and certain block action guns.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Gun mountings permitting recoil or return to battery, e.g. gun cradles; Barrel buffers or brakes -> F41A25/00 - Checking of barrels -> F41A31/02 - Rocket launcher tubes -> F41F3/04
[F41A 21/02] Composite barrels, i.e. barrels having multiple layers, e.g. of different materials -- F41A 21/02 refers to composite barrels used in weapons such as small arms and artillery. These barrels consist of multiple layers of different materials to combine properties like strength, heat resistance, and weight reduction. The classification covers designs where the barrel structure integrates distinct material strata.
[F41A 21/04] Barrel liners -- Barrel liners are removable or permanent inner sleeves installed inside a gun barrel to protect the bore, reduce wear, lessen fouling and enable easier maintenance or caliber conversion. They are commonly made of steel, chrome or polymer and are used in small arms and larger ordnance to extend barrel life and improve performance.
[F41A 21/06] Plural barrels -- This subclass covers weapons that incorporate two or more barrels, such as double-barrel shotguns, twin-tube rocket launchers, or multi-tube artillery pieces. It includes configurations where barrels are side-by-side, stacked or otherwise combined for simultaneous or sequential firing.
[F41A 21/08] Barrel junctions -- The subclass F41A 21/08 covers the design and construction of junctions where two or more weapon barrels are connected to form a single assembly. It includes fittings, couplings or intersecting sections that join barrels of small-arms or artillery, enabling combined or sequential firing and structural integration.
[F41A 21/10] Insert barrels, i.e. barrels for firing reduced calibre ammunition and being mounted within the normal barrels -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Adaptations for training -> F41A33/00
[F41A 21/12] Cartridge chambers -- The F41A 21/12 subclass addresses the design and construction of cartridge chambers, the internal cavity of a cartridge that houses the propellant and projectile. It covers features such as chamber shape, dimensions, sealing methods, and materials that affect ignition, pressure containment, and ballistic performance in small arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 21/14] Arrangement of cartridge chambers lateral to the barrel axis -- F41A 21/14 relates to weapon barrel designs in which the cartridge chambers are placed laterally, off the central barrel axis, a feature used in small-arms and artillery systems.
[F41A 21/16] Barrels or gun tubes characterised by the shape of the bore -- F41A 21/16 covers the design of gun barrels or tubes where the defining feature is the shape of the bore itself. It includes variations such as tapered, stepped, polygonal or other non-cylindrical profiles that affect pressure, gas flow and projectile stabilization in small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 21/18] Grooves-Rifling -- F41A 21/18 covers the design and implementation of grooves or rifling in the bore of firearms and artillery barrels. It relates to the pattern, dimensions, and arrangement of lands and grooves that impart spin to a projectile for stabilisation and accuracy. The classification is used for patents describing barrel interior modifications in small arms and larger ordnance.

[F41A 21/26] specially adapted for recoil reinforcement, e.g. for training purposes -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Includes blank firing arrangements
[F41A 21/28] Gas-expansion chambers -- The CPC class F41A 21/28 covers gas-expansion chambers incorporated into the barrels of firearms and artillery, such as muzzle-devices or recoil-mitigating volumes that use expanding propellant gases to modify pressure or recoil characteristics. It includes designs that influence projectile acceleration, barrel pressure relief, or shock absorption within the barrel system.
[F41A 21/30] Silencers -- F41A 21/30 designates silencers, also called suppressors, that are mounted on or integrated with the barrel of a firearm to reduce the sound and muzzle flash produced when a projectile is fired. The class pertains to functional features common to small-arms and larger ordnance within the mechanical-engineering weapons hierarchy.
[F41A 21/32] Muzzle attachments or glands -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Barrels specially adapted for recoil reinforcement, e.g. for training purposes -> F41A21/26 - Silencers -> F41A21/30 - Barrels having means for separating sabots from projectiles -> F41A21/46 - Tube sights; Bar sights -> F41G1/42
[F41A 21/325] Mountings for muzzle attachments -- The code F41A 21/325 covers devices that attach to the muzzle of a barrel and provide a mounting for accessories such as flash hiders, muzzle brakes or grenade launchers. These mountings are designed to be secured to the barrel end and allow quick installation or removal of the attachment while maintaining barrel alignment.
[F41A 21/34] Flash dampers -- Flash dampers are devices mounted on the barrel of a firearm or artillery piece that reduce the visible and infrared flash produced when a projectile is discharged. By dispersing or extinguishing the hot gases exiting the muzzle, they help conceal the shooter's position and lessen the risk of ignition of nearby flammable material. This functional feature falls under the mechanical-engineering class for weapons, specifically the barrel-related subgroup F41A 21/34.
[F41A 21/36] for recoil reduction -- F41A 21/36 belongs to the CPC section on mechanical engineering for weapons and designates barrel features that provide recoil reduction. It classifies technologies such as muzzle devices, barrel attachments, or internal modifications that lessen the backward force generated when a firearm or artillery piece is discharged.
[F41A 21/38] adjustable -- F41A 21/38 pertains to barrel designs that incorporate adjustable features, such as mechanisms that allow the length, caliber, or other dimensions of a firearm or artillery barrel to be varied after manufacture. Adjustability may be achieved by removable liners, telescoping sections, interchangeable breech inserts, or adjustable-barrel devices, enabling the weapon to be adapted for different performance requirements.
[F41A 21/40] Chokes for shotguns -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Lampel, Mahrholdt, "Waffenlexikon", 9th edition, DE, München, BLV Verlagsgesellschaft, 1981, S. 56  A: normal choke  B: Schlegelmich choke, constricting choke  C: Nischen , jug or recess choke  D: Glockenchoke, bell choke, diverging choke  E, F: Skeet chokes
[F41A 21/42] adjustable -- F41A 21/42 covers barrel designs that can be adjusted, including mechanisms that allow variation of length, caliber, orientation or other parameters to suit different firing conditions.
