[F41A 21/44] Insulation jackets -- Insulation jackets are protective coverings applied to the exterior of weapon barrels to reduce heat buildup, guard against wear, and improve handling. They are used in both small arms and larger ordnance to maintain barrel integrity and operator safety.
[F41A 21/46] Barrels having means for separating sabots from projectiles -- F41A 21/46 classifies barrel designs that include a mechanism for separating sabots from projectiles as the round exits the barrel, a feature used in both small-arms and larger ordnance. The code applies to any barrel where the sabot must be released after the projectile leaves the bore.
[F41A 21/48] Barrel mounting means, e.g. releasable mountings for replaceable barrels -- F41A 21/48 covers means for mounting a firearm barrel that allow it to be released or replaced, including quick-change clamps, locking brackets, and other detachable attachment mechanisms.
[F41A 21/481] using partial or interrupted threads, e.g. bayonet-type mountings -- The code F41A 21/481 designates barrel constructions that employ partial or interrupted threads rather than continuous threading, allowing quick attachment or removal of components such as bayonet-type mountings. This feature is used in both small arms and larger ordnance to simplify assembly and maintenance.
[F41A 21/482] using continuous threads on the barrel -- The code F41A 21/482 relates to barrel designs in small-arms and artillery that employ continuous helical threads along the barrel's surface. It covers the use of such threads for securely mounting, attaching, or adjusting accessories, muzzle devices, or barrel extensions. This feature is classified under functional details common to both firearms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 21/484] using interlocking means, e.g. by sliding pins -- F41A 21/484 covers barrel designs that are secured by interlocking means such as sliding pins. This method creates a mechanical lock without separate fasteners, simplifying assembly and enhancing the structural integrity of small-arms and ordnance barrels.
[F41A 21/485] using screws or bolts -- F41A 21/485 covers barrel constructions for small arms or ordnance in which the barrel components are assembled or secured by screws or bolts rather than by welding, casting, or other permanent joining methods. It includes designs where threaded fasteners provide alignment, reinforcement, or modularity for barrel sections, breech blocks, or mounting hardware. This subclass is used to index patents that detail screw- or bolt-based barrel attachment techniques.
[F41A 21/487] using friction, e.g. by clamping a barrel surface -- The subclass covers techniques that employ friction to secure or control a barrel, for example by clamping the barrel surface to limit movement, improve alignment or enhance stability. It applies to both small-arms and larger ordnance where barrel fixation by friction is relevant.
[F41A 21/488] Mountings specially adapted for pistols or revolvers -- Mountings designed specifically for hand-held firearms such as pistols or revolvers, including brackets, grips, or other attachment devices that secure the weapon to a supporting structure or accessory.
[F41A 23/00] Gun mountings, e.g. on vehicles -- **Definition:** This place covers:  All mountings of firearms, fixed or mobile, except for the ones specifically designed for return to battery and the ones permitting traversing or elevating movement (see precedence, F41A25/00 and F41A27/00 ). It includes locking means between gun an mounting, mountings without wheels (conventional mono-, bi- or tripods, test mounts and bench rests), mountings permitting the gun to retract into a hidden position, wheeled mountings (the wheels being mounted directly on the gun), mounted on vehicles, mounts for transport and adjustments means of the platform position.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Gun mountings permitting recoil or return to battery, e.g. gun cradles; Barrel buffers or brakes -> F41A25/00 - Gun mountings permitting traversing or elevating movement, e.g. gun carriages -> F41A27/00
[F41A 23/005] Locks for connecting guns to their mountings  F41A23/50  takes precedence -- This subclass covers locking devices that secure guns-both small arms and larger ordnance-to their mountings on vehicles, ships or other platforms, including clamps, pins, bolts and similar mechanisms that hold the weapon in a fixed firing position.
[F41A 23/02] Mountings without wheels -- F41A 23/02 refers to gun mountings that are fixed or lack wheels, used to attach small-arms or ordnance to vehicles or other platforms without providing wheeled mobility.
[F41A 23/04] Unipods -- F41A 23/04 covers unipods, single-leg support devices that attach to firearms or artillery to provide a simple stabilising mount, often used on vehicle-mounted weapons or portable guns. The classification addresses the structural features that enable the weapon to be steadied with one leg for improved aiming and firing accuracy.
[F41A 23/06] adjustable -- F41A 23/06 pertains to adjustable gun mountings, primarily for weapons such as cannons and small-arms installed on vehicles, where the mounting can be varied in orientation, elevation or recoil characteristics. The subclass addresses mechanical features that allow the mounting to be altered to suit different firing conditions.
[F41A 23/08] Bipods -- The CPC subclass F41A 23/08 covers bipods, two-legged support devices that attach to firearms or artillery to stabilize the weapon and improve accuracy, especially on vehicle-mounted guns. It belongs to the mechanical engineering weapons group dealing with functional features common to both small arms and ordnance.
[F41A 23/10] adjustable -- F41A 23/10 covers adjustable gun mountings, such as those used to secure weapons on vehicles, allowing the firearm's orientation, elevation or recoil travel to be varied to suit different tactical situations. The classification groups these adjustable features within mechanical-engineering weapon systems for small-arms and ordnance.
[F41A 23/12] Tripods -- F41A 23/12 designates tripods employed as gun mountings, especially for weapons installed on vehicles or other platforms. The subclass includes the structure, adjustment mechanisms and attachment methods of tripod supports that provide stable aiming and firing for both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 23/14] adjustable -- F41A 23/14 relates to gun mountings that can be adjusted in elevation, traverse, recoil or other positions to match varying firing conditions, commonly applied to vehicle-borne weapons such as tanks, self-propelled guns or other mobile artillery.
[F41A 23/16] Testing mounts -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Rests holding the firearm so as to enable stabilised shooting, includes both test shooting mounts and shooting rests, i.e. for bench rest shooting
[F41A 23/18] Rests for supporting smallarms in non-shooting position -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Rests for holding the firearm in a fixed position for displaying, inspecting, adjusting, maintaining, repairing or cleaning of the firearm.
[F41A 23/20] for disappearing guns -- The F41A 23/20 subclass covers gun-mounting arrangements that enable the weapon to be withdrawn or concealed after firing, such as retractable or disappearing mounts used on fortifications, coastal batteries and vehicle-mounted artillery. It includes mechanisms that lower the gun into a protected position or hide it behind armor or earthworks to reduce visibility and vulnerability. This functional feature applies to both small-arms and larger ordnance systems.
[F41A 23/22] on board of submarines -- F41A 23/22 pertains to the design and integration of weapon mounts installed on submarines, covering mechanisms that secure guns or launchers to the hull or interior. It addresses the structural and functional features required for stable operation of armament under underwater conditions.
[F41A 23/24] Turret gun mountings -- F41A 23/24 classifies turret gun mountings, the structural and mechanical arrangements for attaching and supporting a firearm on a rotating turret of a vehicle. The code covers the mounting architecture, elevation and traverse mechanisms, recoil management, and integration with the vehicle's chassis or hull.
[F41A 23/26] Mountings for transport only -- F41A 23/26 covers mountings that support firearms or artillery solely for transport, such as vehicle brackets, racks or sleds that hold the weapon while moving but are not intended for firing or recoil absorption. These fittings secure the weapon during transit and are often detachable, allowing rapid deployment.
[F41A 23/28] Wheeled-gun mountings -- **Definition:** This place covers:  The wheels are directly mounted on the gun or gun cradle.
[F41A 23/30] the wheels being liftable from the ground for firing -- F41A 23/30 classifies vehicle-mounted gun systems whose wheels can be lifted from the ground while firing to improve stability and manage recoil. It covers hydraulic, pneumatic or mechanical devices that raise the carriage wheels or tracks during the firing sequence.
[F41A 23/32] with split trails -- A gun mounting arrangement that uses two separate, spread-able trail legs to support a weapon, allowing the trails to be opened for stability and closed for transport, typically employed on vehicle-mounted artillery or field guns.
[F41A 23/34] on wheeled or endless-track vehicles
[F41A 23/36] on trailers -- F41A 23/36 covers gun mountings that are built onto or attached to trailers, enabling firearms or artillery pieces to be transported and sometimes operated directly from a trailer platform. The classification addresses the structural and functional aspects of these mobile mounting systems used in military and related applications.
[F41A 23/38] on motorcycles
[F41A 23/40] on rail vehicles -- The CPC subclass F41A 23/40 covers gun mountings that are installed on rail vehicles, including railway artillery platforms, train-mounted cannons and other weapons carried on locomotives or carriages. It addresses functional features of weapons adapted for railway use.
[F41A 23/42] for rocket throwers -- F41A 23/42 pertains to mountings designed to support rocket launchers, especially when installed on vehicles or other platforms. It falls under functional features common to both small arms and ordnance, covering gun-mounting arrangements for rocket throwers.
[F41A 23/44] on sledges -- The CPC entry F41A 23/44 concerns the mounting of guns or other weapons on sled-type carriers. It covers designs and arrangements that attach firearms, cannons or similar ordnance to sleds used for transport, positioning or firing, often as part of vehicle-borne or mobile weapon systems.
[F41A 23/46] Trail spades -- **Definition:** This place covers:  GB2455651
[F41A 23/48] elastic -- The CPC subclass F41A 23/48 covers elastic components in gun mountings for vehicle-borne weapons, such as flexible or shock-absorbing devices that permit relative movement of the weapon while dampening vibration and recoil forces.
[F41A 23/50] Travelling locks -- F41A 23/50 covers travelling locks, which are mechanisms that secure a gun mounting while permitting limited movement, allowing weapons on vehicles to remain aligned during transport and to be quickly positioned for firing.
[F41A 23/52] Base plates for gun mountings -- Base plates for gun mountings are structural components that secure a firearm to a vehicle or platform, distributing recoil forces and providing a stable, level foundation for accurate firing. Classified under CPC F41A 23/52, they belong to the functional features common to small arms and ordnance within mechanical engineering.
[F41A 23/54] for mortars -- F41A 23/54 covers mounting arrangements that attach mortar weapons to vehicles, including brackets, recoil mechanisms and support structures required for stable firing. The classification addresses the design and integration of these mounts within vehicle platforms, focusing on functional features common to both small arms and ordnance.
[F41A 23/56] Arrangements for adjusting the gun platform in the vertical or horizontal position -- The F41A 23/56 classification addresses mechanisms that permit vertical elevation or horizontal traverse adjustments of a gun platform, such as the devices used to raise, lower, or swivel weapons mounted on vehicles. It pertains to functional features common to both small-arms and larger ordnance within the mechanical-engineering weapons field.
[F41A 23/58] Hydraulic jacks -- The F41A 23/58 subclass designates hydraulic jack mechanisms employed in gun mountings, such as those fitted to vehicles for small-arms and larger ordnance. These devices provide lifting, positioning or recoil-absorbing functions to support and operate the weapon system.
[F41A 23/60] Screw-operated jacks -- F41A 23/60 relates to screw-operated jacks used to support or adjust the position of weapons such as guns mounted on vehicles. The subclass covers devices that raise, lower, or tilt a gun mount by converting rotary motion of a screw into linear movement, providing stable, controllable positioning for small arms or ordnance.
[F41A 25/00] Gun mountings permitting recoil or return to battery, e.g. gun cradles -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Gun mounts permitting travel of the gun from the shooting position into full recoil position along a bearing and returning to battery position, including spring, fluid, friction operated systems as well as hybrids thereof. It further includes bearings and assembly/disassembly features peculiar thereto.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Recoilless guns, i.e. guns having propulsion means producing no recoil -> F41A1/08
[F41A 25/02] Fluid-operated systems -- F41A 25/02 covers fluid-operated mechanisms incorporated in gun mountings that manage recoil and return the weapon to its firing position, such as hydraulic or pneumatic cylinders used in cradles for cannons or small arms. This subclass addresses the design of systems that harness liquid or gas pressure to absorb shock and reset the gun after discharge.
[F41A 25/04] adjustable -- F41A 25/04 refers to gun mountings that incorporate adjustable features for recoil control or return-to-battery, such as cradles whose position, angle or tension can be varied to suit different weapons. The classification covers mechanisms that allow fine tuning of mounting parameters to optimise firing stability and accuracy.
[F41A 25/06] Friction-operated systems -- F41A 25/06 covers friction-operated mechanisms employed in gun mountings that control recoil or return the weapon to its firing position, such as cradles for cannons and small arms. The class pertains to functional features common to both small-arms and ordnance, focusing on systems that use friction to manage and absorb recoil forces.
[F41A 25/08] adjustable -- F41A 25/08 covers gun mounting systems that can be adjusted to modify recoil or return-to-battery positions, allowing the cradle or support to be set to different angles or heights for optimal alignment with the weapon.
[F41A 25/10] Spring-operated systems -- F41A 25/10 covers spring-operated mechanisms used in gun mountings that control recoil or return the weapon to its firing position, such as spring-driven cradles and other recoil-absorbing or battery-return systems found on small arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 25/12] using coil springs -- The subclass covers gun mountings that use coil springs to absorb recoil or return the weapon to its firing position. Coil springs serve as a compact, reusable energy-storage element in cradles or carriages, providing controlled recoil damping and rapid resetting.
[F41A 25/14] adjustable -- The F41A 25/14 class pertains to adjustable gun-mounting systems, such as cradles or recoil-and-return-to-battery devices, that can be set to different positions or angles to alter alignment, elevation or recoil control for cannons and similar large firearms.
[F41A 25/16] Hybrid systems -- Hybrid systems refer to gun mounting arrangements that integrate two or more types of recoil-absorbing technologies, such as combining hydraulic, pneumatic, or spring mechanisms to control recoil and return the weapon to battery. They are used in both small arms and larger ordnance where a single method would be insufficient for the forces involved. This classification covers the design and application of such combined-action recoil devices.
[F41A 25/18] Hydroelastic systems -- Hydroelastic systems are mechanisms that employ fluid-filled elastic elements to absorb and control recoil in gun mountings, enabling the weapon to return smoothly to its firing position. They are used in artillery and large-caliber mounts to manage recoil forces and improve stability.
[F41A 25/20] Hydropneumatic systems -- F41A 25/20 relates to hydropneumatic systems used in gun mountings that control recoil and return the weapon to its firing position, such as hydraulic-pneumatic buffers and recuperators for cannons, artillery pieces and small-arms platforms.
[F41A 25/22] Bearing arrangements for the reciprocating gun-mount or barrel movement -- The subclass covers bearing systems that support and guide the reciprocating motion of a gun mount or barrel during recoil and return to battery, detailing bearing types, lubrication methods and load-distribution arrangements for such weapons.
[F41A 25/24] using ball or roller bearings -- F41A 25/24 covers the incorporation of ball or roller bearings into gun mountings that allow recoil absorption or automatic return to battery, such as cradles for cannons and small arms. This functional feature improves the smoothness and durability of the recoil mechanism within weapons engineering.
[F41A 25/26] Assembling or dismounting recoil elements or systems -- The subclass F41A 25/26 covers methods and apparatus for assembling or removing recoil-related components or whole recoil systems in gun mountings such as cradles that allow a weapon to recoil and return to battery. It is part of the mechanical-engineering hierarchy for weapons, focusing on functional features common to both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 27/00] Gun mountings permitting traversing or elevating movement, e.g. gun carriages -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Mechanical aspects of gun mounts permitting supported pointing of the gun for laying the gun, i.e. in the position to fire the gun and hitting the target.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Monopods -> F41A23/04 - Bipods -> F41A23/08 - Tripods -> F41A23/12 - Aiming means; Laying means -> F41G3/00 - Elevation or traversing control systems for guns -> F41G5/00
[F41A 27/02] Control systems for preventing interference between the moving gun and the adjacent structure -- F41A 27/02 covers control systems that detect and prevent collisions or interference between a gun in motion and nearby structural components of its mounting, such as the carriage, turret or platform. These systems are used on traversing or elevating weapon mounts to ensure safe operation and avoid damage to the gun or its support structure.
[F41A 27/04] Scatter-fire arrangements, i.e. means for oscillating guns automatically during firing

[F41A 27/08] Bearings, e.g. trunnions -- The CPC subclass F41A 27/08 relates to bearings employed in gun mountings, such as trunnions that support and enable a weapon's barrel to pivot for elevation and traverse. [F41A 27/10] Bearings for supporting a pivoting gun in a wall, e.g. a turret wall -- A bearing designed to support a gun that pivots within a wall, such as a turret wall, enabling the weapon to be traversed or elevated. [F41A 27/12] Brakes or locks for blocking traversing or elevating gear in a fixed position -- F41A 27/12 covers mechanisms that lock or brake the traversing or elevating gear of gun mountings, keeping the weapon in a fixed position during operation or transport.
[F41A 27/14] Central-pivot bearings -- F41A 27/14 Central-pivot bearings support a weapon's barrel or turret on a single central axis, allowing horizontal traversing and vertical elevation. They are used in gun carriages and similar mountings where precise movement of the gun is required.
[F41A 27/16] using raceway bearings, e.g. for supporting the turret -- F41A 27/16 covers gun mountings that employ raceway bearings to support a turret, providing smooth traversing or elevating motion. The subclass is part of the mechanical engineering weapons group dealing with functional features common to small-arms and ordnance such as gun carriages.
[F41A 27/18] for gun turrets -- F41A 27/18 designates gun turrets, the mounting assemblies that enable a weapon to traverse horizontally and elevate vertically. It covers the structural and mechanical features used in naval, armored-vehicle or other platforms where a cannon or large-calibre firearm is positioned for controlled aiming.
[F41A 27/20] Drives for turret movements -- The CPC code F41A 27/20 falls under mechanical engineering weapons functional features common to small arms and ordnance and concerns gun mountings that allow traversing or elevating movement such as carriages. It specifically covers drive mechanisms that power turret movements, including hydraulic, electric or pneumatic systems used to rotate or elevate a gun turret.
[F41A 27/22] Traversing gear -- F41A 27/22 covers the traversing gear used in gun mountings such as carriages that allow a cannon or small-arm to be rotated or elevated for aiming. [F41A 3/00] Breech mechanisms, e.g. locks -- **Definition:** This place covers:  The breech mechanism, i.e. the breech block itself and the mechanism enabling the opening and closing and if applicable the locking of the breech. Furthermore the mounting of the breech block and mechanism is covered.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Mechanisms or systems operated by propellant charge energy for automatically opening the lock -> F41A5/00 - Auxiliary mechanisms for bringing the breech-block or bolt or the barrel to the starting position before automatic firing -> F41A7/00 - Ramming arrangements, the breech-block itself being the rammer -> F41A9/40 - Mounting of cartridge ejectors on or within the bolt -> F41A15/14 - Mounting of cartridges ejectors in the breech housing -> F41A15/16 - Safeties for locking the breech-block or bolt in a safety position -> F41A17/42  **Glossary:** - Breech: The rear part of the bore of a gun barrel, especially the opening that permits the projectile to be inserted at the rear of the bore.
[F41A 3/02] Block action, i.e. the main breech opening movement being transverse to the barrel axis -- F41A 3/02 covers block-type breech mechanisms where the primary opening movement of the breech block travels across the barrel axis, as used in cannons and small-arms. The classification is applied to weapons whose breech action slides laterally to expose the chamber for loading.
[F41A 3/04] with pivoting breech-block -- The CPC subclass F41A 3/04 covers breech mechanisms for small arms and ordnance that use a pivoting breech-block to close and seal the chamber during firing. This design lets the block swing on a hinge, enabling quick opening and secure locking, and is found in rifles, cannons and similar weapons.
[F41A 3/06] about a horizontal axis transverse to the barrel axis at the rear of the block -- F41A 3/06 covers breech-lock mechanisms in firearms and artillery where a horizontal axis, positioned at the rear of the breech block, runs across the barrel axis, allowing the block to pivot sideways for opening and closing the chamber. This design is used in both small arms and larger ordnance to facilitate loading and extraction.
[F41A 3/08] carrying a rotably mounted obturating plug of the screw-thread or the interrupted-thread type -- F41A 3/08 covers breech mechanisms in weapons that use a rotably mounted obturating plug with a screw-thread or interrupted-thread design to seal the chamber during firing. The plug rotates to engage the threads, providing a gas-tight closure for small arms or artillery cannons. This feature is typical of lock or breechblock systems that require reliable obturation.
[F41A 3/10] with sliding breech-block, e.g. vertically -- F41A 3/10 covers breech mechanisms that use a sliding breech-block, typically moving vertically, for small-arms and artillery. The class includes designs where the block slides to open and close the chamber, providing sealing and loading functions.
[F41A 31/00] Testing arrangements -- **Definition:** This place covers:  All arrangements, devices, systems for testing the functionality of a firearm, except for testing of sighting, aiming and laying means.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Testing mounts -> F41A23/16
[F41A 31/02] for checking gun barrels -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Devices for testing or checking of sights (parrallelity with the barrel) -> F41G1/54
[F41A 3/12] Bolt action, i.e. the main breech opening movement being parallel to the barrel axis -- F41A 3/12 covers bolt-action firearms, where the primary breech opening moves parallel to the barrel axis. It includes mechanisms in rifles and similar weapons that use a sliding bolt to lock, unlock, and chamber cartridges.
[F41A 3/14] Rigid bolt locks, i.e. having locking elements rigidly mounted on the bolt or bolt handle and on the barrel or breech-housing respectively -- F41A 3/14 designates rigid bolt locks for firearms, where the locking elements are fixed directly to the bolt or its handle and to the barrel or breech housing, creating a secure lock during firing. This subclass falls under functional features common to both small arms and ordnance, specifically breech mechanisms.
[F41A 3/16] the locking elements effecting a rotary movement about the barrel axis, e.g. rotating cylinder bolt locks
[F41A 3/18] hand-operated -- This subclass covers hand-operated breech mechanisms and locks used in firearms and artillery, where the operator manually opens and closes the breech. It includes lever, wheel, or crank actions for loading and firing.
[F41A 3/20] Straight-pull operated bolt locks, i.e. the operating hand effecting only a straight movement parallel to the barrel axis -- F41A 3/20 covers straight-pull bolt-lock mechanisms where the shooter's hand moves the bolt directly forward or backward along the barrel axis without rotation. These locks are used in firearms and artillery as a breech-closing system, simplifying operation compared with traditional rotating bolts.
[F41A 3/22] the locking being effected by rotating the operating handle or lever transversely to the barrel axis -- The subclass covers firearms whose breech lock is engaged by rotating a handle or lever that moves transversely across the barrel axis, rather than by longitudinal motion. This transverse actuation secures the breech during firing and applies to both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 3/24] the locking elements forming part of the operating handle or lever -- The CPC subclass F41A 3/24 covers the locking elements that are built into the operating handle or lever of breech mechanisms for small-arms and ordnance. It specifies the components that secure the breech during opening and closing, a functional feature common to rifles, cannons and similar weapons.
[F41A 3/26] semi-automatically or automatically operated, e.g. having a slidable bolt-carrier and a rotatable bolt -- This subclass covers breech mechanisms of weapons that operate semi-automatically or automatically, typically using a sliding bolt-carrier and a rotating bolt to chamber and lock each round. It applies to small-arms and larger ordnance where the action cycles without manual intervention for each shot.
[F41A 3/28] having fixed locking elements on the non-rotating bolt and rotating locking elements mounted on the barrel or breech housing, e.g. rotatable rings -- F41A 3/28 addresses breech mechanisms that use fixed locking elements on a non-rotating bolt together with rotating locking parts mounted on the barrel or breech housing, such as rotatable rings, to secure the lock-up in small-arms and ordnance. This combination provides a reliable method of transmitting firing forces while allowing the bolt to stay stationary.
[F41A 3/30] Interlocking means, e.g. locking lugs, screw threads -- The CPC class F41A 3/30 covers interlocking means such as locking lugs and screw threads used in breech mechanisms of weapons, ensuring secure closure of the firing chamber. It falls under mechanical engineering, weapons, functional features common to small arms and ordnance.
[F41A 33/00] Adaptations for training -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Adaptations of firearms for use in training without live ammunition or weapons specially adapted for training and simulation only.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Insert barrels -> F41A21/10 - Adaptations of barrels for recoil reinforcement -> F41A21/26 - Shooting or hurling games -> A63F9/02 - Teaching or practice apparatus for gun-aiming or gun-laying -> F41G3/26 - Simulators for teaching or training purposes for military purposes and tactics -> G09B9/003
[F41A 33/02] Light- or radiation-emitting guns -- **Definition:** This place covers: Includes: separate light producing units fitted in the barrel of an existing gun and externally mounted light beam emitting units (torch-like)  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** This place does not cover: Cartridges carrying light emitting sources F42B8/00 Military or tactic simulation systems G09B9/003  **Glossary:** In this place, the following terms or expressions are used with the meaning indicated: MILES: Multiple Integrated Laser Engagement System
[F41A 33/04] Acoustical simulation of gun fire, e.g. by pyrotechnic means -- F41A 33/04 covers technologies that reproduce the acoustic signature of gun discharge for training purposes, such as pyrotechnic blanks or other sound-generating devices. The classification resides in the Mechanical Engineering weapons section, focusing on functional features common to small arms and ordnance used in training simulations.
[F41A 33/06] Recoil simulators -- F41A 33/06 covers recoil simulators, devices that reproduce the kick-back of small arms or ordnance for training purposes. These systems use mechanical, pneumatic or hydraulic means to mimic the recoil impulse of weapons such as rifles, pistols or cannons, allowing realistic firing practice without live ammunition. They are employed in shooting ranges, simulators and instructional facilities to teach handling, aiming and recoil management.
[F41A 3/32] the bolt being rocked about a notional axis transverse to the barrel axis -- This subclass covers firearms whose bolt is moved by rocking it around an imaginary axis that runs across the barrel rather than sliding straight back. The motion pivots the bolt sideways to lock or unlock the breech, typical of certain breech-block or lock-type weapons.
[F41A 3/34] the bolt additionally effecting a sliding movement transverse to the barrel axis -- F41A 3/34 classifies breech mechanisms in firearms where the bolt, besides its normal rearward motion, also moves laterally across the barrel axis. This transverse sliding can be used to lock, unlock, or index the bolt, improving sealing or allowing multi-chamber operation.
[F41A 3/36] Semi-rigid bolt locks, i.e. having locking elements movably mounted on the bolt or on the barrel or breech housing -- F41A 3/36 refers to semi-rigid bolt lock mechanisms in which the locking elements are movably mounted on the bolt, barrel or breech housing and can be shifted to secure the breech, a design used in both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 3/38] having rocking locking elements, e.g. pivoting levers or vanes -- F41A 3/38 covers breech mechanisms for firearms and artillery that employ rocking locking elements, such as pivoting levers or vanes, to secure the breech during firing. The classification applies to designs where these movable components rotate or swing into place to lock the chamber, providing a reliable seal and enabling controlled discharge.
[F41A 3/40] mounted on the bolt -- The F41A 3/40 subclass covers breech-action weapons whose lock or operating components are attached directly to the bolt rather than to the barrel or other parts of the firearm. It includes designs where the firing or cocking mechanisms are mounted on the moving bolt, allowing the bolt to perform both sealing and actuation functions during the loading and firing cycle. This configuration is typical of certain small-arms and artillery breech mechanisms.
[F41A 3/42] hand-operated -- The CPC subclass F41A 3/42 covers hand-operated breech mechanisms or locks used in firearms and artillery, where the breech is opened and closed manually by the operator. It includes manual latches, lever-type locks and other manually actuated devices that secure the chamber for loading and firing.
[F41A 3/44] having sliding locking elements, e.g. balls, rollers -- F41A 3/44 pertains to breech mechanisms for small arms and ordnance that employ sliding locking elements-such as balls or rollers-to engage and secure the breech during firing, offering rapid and reliable lock-up.
[F41A 3/46] mounted on the bolt -- F41A 3/46 designates breech mechanisms in firearms or artillery where the functional component-such as a lock, latch or safety device-is mounted directly on the bolt. This arrangement contrasts with systems that attach the mechanism to the chamber or other parts of the weapon.
[F41A 3/48] hand-operated -- F41A 3/48 designates hand-operated breech mechanisms, covering manually actuated locks and opening devices used in both small-arms and larger ordnance such as cannons. It includes any manual means for securing, unlocking or rotating the breech block during loading and firing.
[F41A 3/50] Toggle-joint locks, e.g. crank-operated -- Toggle-joint locks, such as those operated by a crank, are a type of breech-opening mechanism used in firearms and artillery. The crank rotates a lever that pivots at a joint, moving the breech block to seal or release the chamber for loading and firing.
[F41A 35/00] Accessories or details not otherwise provided for -- F41A 35/00 covers accessories, fittings or detail components for weapons that are not specifically defined in other classes, such as miscellaneous mounts, safety devices, or handling aids applicable to both small arms and larger ordnance. It is used when a feature does not fall under a more specific subclass in the weapons hierarchy.
[F41A 35/02] Dust- or weather-protection caps or covers -- F41A 35/02 covers dust- or weather-protection caps and covers designed for weapons, including small arms and ordnance, that shield the barrel, breech or other functional parts from environmental elements. These accessories are intended to prevent contamination, corrosion or moisture ingress during storage or transport.
[F41A 35/04] Muzzle covers -- Muzzle covers are accessories fitted over the barrel end of firearms, including small arms and larger ordnance, to protect the muzzle from damage, debris and environmental exposure. They may also help control flash or prevent accidental ignition when the weapon is not in use. This functional feature falls under the mechanical engineering weapons subclass for common details.
[F41A 35/06] Adaptation of guns to both right and left hand use -- This classification covers design features that enable a firearm to be operated equally well by right- and left-hand users, including reversible safeties, interchangeable grips, and controls positioned for ambidextrous use. It applies to weapons such as pistols, rifles and cannons where the adaptation allows the gun to be handled comfortably and safely by either hand.
[F41A 3/52] hand-operated -- F41A 3/52 covers hand-operated breech mechanisms and locks used in small arms and artillery, where the user manually opens and closes the breech by pulling, turning or sliding a lever or bolt. The classification includes manual screw, lever and other hand-actuated locking devices for loading and firing.
[F41A 3/54] Bolt locks of the unlocked type, i.e. being inertia operated -- F41A 3/54 classifies inertia-operated bolt locks that remain unlocked until the recoil of a firearm forces them to close, a mechanism used in both small arms and larger ordnance. It falls under the mechanical-engineering weapons hierarchy, specifically the functional features of breech mechanisms. This entry captures designs that rely on the weapon's own motion to lock the breech.
[F41A 3/56] the bolt being provided with an additional slidable mass -- F41A 3/56 covers breech-loading firearms where the bolt incorporates an extra, movable mass that can slide relative to the bolt body. The additional mass is used to balance recoil forces, improve lock-up, or provide a delayed-blowback action in small-arms and artillery breech mechanisms.
[F41A 3/58] Breakdown breech mechanisms, e.g. for shotguns -- The entry pertains to breakdown breech mechanisms employed in firearms, notably shotguns, where the barrel is hinged to swing open for loading and unloading of ammunition.
[F41A 3/60] Breech mechanisms for guns having two or more barrels -- F41A 3/60 designates breech mechanisms used in guns that have two or more barrels, such as double-barrel firearms. The classification covers the lock, opening, closing and sealing features that allow each barrel to be loaded and fired independently or sequentially.
[F41A 3/62] using combustion gas pressure for adding to the mechanical locking action, or for delaying breech opening movement -- F41A 3/62 covers breech mechanisms in firearms and artillery that employ the pressure of combustion gases to supplement the mechanical lock or to retard the opening of the breech. The feature is used to enhance safety or control the timing of the firing cycle by using gas-driven assistance.
[F41A 3/64] Mounting of breech-blocks -- F41A 3/64 addresses the ways in which the breech block of a firearm or artillery piece is attached to the weapon, covering fixtures, hinges, locking lugs and other mounting structures that enable opening-and-closing of the breech for loading.
[F41A 3/66] Breech housings or frames -- F41A 3/66 relates to the housings or frames that form the structural support for breech assemblies of small arms and larger ordnance, enclosing the locking mechanisms and sealing the chamber against gas pressure. These components are designed to withstand firing stresses while enabling loading and extraction of cartridges.
[F41A 3/68] Bolt stops, i.e. means for limiting bolt opening movement
[F41A 3/70] Anti-rebound arrangements, i.e. preventing rebound of the bolt out of the firing position
[F41A 3/72] Operating handles or levers -- F41A 3/72 covers the design and arrangement of operating handles or levers that actuate breech mechanisms in firearms and artillery. These components transmit the shooter's or operator's force to open, close, or lock the breech, enabling loading and firing.
[F41A 3/74] Obturating or packing devices for gas leak prevention in breech mechanisms -- F41A 3/74 covers obturating or packing devices that seal the breech of firearms and artillery to prevent propellant-gas leakage during firing. It includes seals, obturators, gaskets and related packing materials designed to improve safety, pressure containment and performance of small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 3/76] specially adapted for sealing the gap between the forward end of the cartridge chamber and the rearward end of the barrel, e.g. sealing devices for revolvers or revolver-type guns -- This CPC subclass deals with breech mechanisms that are specially adapted to seal the gap between the forward end of the cartridge chamber and the rearward end of the barrel, such as sealing devices used in revolvers or revolver-type firearms. It falls under mechanical engineering weapons functional features common to small arms and ordnance.
[F41A 3/78] Bolt buffer or recuperator means -- **Definition:** This place covers:  The primary mechanism in automatic and semi-automatic firearms for bringing the bolt or breech block back into closed position after a recoil cycle.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Auxiliary mechanisms for bringing the breech-block or bolt or the barrel to the starting position before automatic firing -> F41A7/00 - Gun mounting permitting recoil or return to battery -> F41A25/00
[F41A 3/80] Adjustable spring buffers -- F41A 3/80 pertains to adjustable spring buffers incorporated in breech mechanisms of firearms and artillery, providing a tunable means to moderate recoil or opening forces. The buffers can be set to varying stiffness levels to control the speed of breech closure and reduce mechanical wear.
[F41A 3/82] Coil spring buffers -- F41A 3/82 relates to coil-spring buffers employed in breech mechanisms of firearms and artillery. The classification covers devices that use a coiled spring to absorb recoil or cushion the motion of moving parts, enhancing safety and performance in small arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 3/84] mounted within the gun stock -- F41A 3/84 covers breech mechanisms that are mounted within the stock of a firearm, encompassing designs where the lock or breech components are integrated inside the gun stock rather than positioned externally.
[F41A 3/86] mounted under the barrel -- This subclass concerns breech-locking or loading mechanisms that are situated beneath the barrel of weapons such as small-arms or cannons. It includes designs where the lock, hinge or other functional elements are mounted under the barrel to secure the breech and assist firing or reloading.
[F41A 3/88] mounted around the barrel -- F41A 3/88 designates breech mechanisms, such as locks, that are mounted around the barrel of firearms or artillery. The subclass groups devices that encircle the barrel to provide sealing, loading or firing functions and is within the broader weapons and mechanical engineering categories.
[F41A 3/90] Fluid buffers -- F41A 3/90 designates fluid buffers used in breech mechanisms of weapons such as small arms and artillery. These buffers employ hydraulic or pneumatic systems to absorb recoil forces and reduce stress on the breech components, improving reliability and safety.
[F41A 3/92] adjustable -- F41A 3/92 designates adjustable breech-mechanism features used in weapons such as small arms and cannons. It covers elements that can be varied-by length, angle or tension-to suit different ammunition, firing pressures or operating conditions, ensuring proper lock engagement and timing. This subclass falls under functional details common to both smallarms and ordnance.
[F41A 3/94] in combination with spring buffers -- F41A 3/94 covers breech-lock arrangements for firearms and artillery in which the locking mechanism is used together with spring buffers that absorb recoil or impact forces. It applies to both small-arms and larger ordnance where such combined spring-buffer systems are incorporated into the breech design.
[F41A 5/00] Mechanisms or systems operated by propellant charge energy for automatically opening the lock -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Mechanisms for opening the lock of semi-automatic and automatic firearms using ONLY the energy provided by the propellant charge during the launching of the projectile, i.e. autonomous systems, independent from an auxiliary source of energy.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Auxiliary mechanisms for bringing the breech-block or bolt or the barrel to the starting position before automatic firing -> F41A7/00 - Internally powered drives for feeding and loading ammunition -> F41A9/49 - Externally power or control systems -> F41A9/50
[F41A 5/02] recoil-operated -- F41A 5/02 covers recoil-operated mechanisms in weapons, where the energy from a propellant charge is used to automatically open the breech or lock after firing. It applies to both small-arms and larger ordnance such as cannons, describing the functional features that harness recoil to cycle the action.
[F41A 5/04] the barrel being tilted during recoil -- F41A 5/04 classifies weapon mechanisms in which the barrel tilts backward as recoil energy is absorbed, automatically moving the lock to a cocked position. This functional feature applies to small-arms and ordnance that use propellant charge energy to prepare the firing mechanism for the next shot.
[F41A 5/06] the barrel being rotated about its longitudinal axis during recoil -- The F41A 5/06 classification covers firearms in which the barrel twists around its own length as the weapon recoils, using the energy of the propellant charge to drive an automatic lock-opening mechanism. This functional feature applies to both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 5/08] having an accelerator lever acting on the breech-block or bolt during the opening movement -- F41A 5/08 designates mechanisms that use an accelerator lever to push or pull the breech-block or bolt while it is being opened, employing the energy of a propellant charge to speed the unlocking movement. This functional feature is common to both small-arms and larger ordnance that rely on automatic opening of the lock. It falls under mechanical engineering of weapons, specifically the functional details of propellant-driven opening systems.
[F41A 5/10] having a movable inertia weight -- **Definition:** This place covers:  The energy is accumulated during the rearward of the bolt or barrel after the lock has been opened by the recoil or due to gas operation, consequently implying that the stored energy is used for closing the lock
[F41A 5/12] mounted in a gun having a fixed barrel -- F41A 5/12 covers mechanisms or systems that use the energy of a propellant charge to automatically open the lock of a gun that has a fixed barrel, such as a cannon or other large-calibre weapon. It includes devices that are mounted on the gun itself to perform the opening action without manual intervention.
[F41A 5/14] Barrel stops, i.e. devices for holding the recoiling barrel in a predetermined position, e.g. the recoil position -- F41A 5/14 covers barrel-stop mechanisms for firearms and artillery that use propellant energy, providing devices that hold the recoiling barrel in a set position such as the recoil position, thereby controlling barrel movement after discharge.
[F41A 5/16] having a barrel moving forwardly after the firing of a shot -- The code F41A 5/16 designates weapons whose barrel is driven forward automatically after a shot is fired, a feature typical of recoil-operated firearms that use the propellant's energy to cycle the action and ready the next round. This functional detail applies to both small arms and larger ordnance where barrel movement is part of the firing mechanism.
[F41A 5/18] gas-operated -- The F41A 5/18 subclass covers mechanisms or systems that employ the energy of a propellant gas charge to automatically open a weapon's lock, a functional feature common to both small-arms and larger ordnance such as cannons.
[F41A 5/20] using a gas piston arranged concentrically around the barrel -- F41A 5/20 covers firearm mechanisms that employ a gas piston positioned concentrically around the barrel to capture propellant gases and use that energy to automatically open the lock. It pertains to functional features common to small-arms and ordnance, such as cannons and other weapons.
[F41A 5/22] having two or more gas pistons -- This subclass covers weapon mechanisms that use the energy of a propellant charge to automatically open the breech or lock, specifically designs that incorporate two or more gas pistons to drive the opening action.
[F41A 5/24] by direct action of gas pressure on bolt or locking elements -- F41A 5/24 covers firearm mechanisms that use the direct pressure of propellant gases to move the bolt or other locking components, causing the lock to open automatically without intermediate mechanical links. This principle is applied in small-arms and larger ordnance where rapid unlocking is required.
[F41A 5/26] Arrangements or systems for bleeding the gas from the barrel -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Gas operated mechanisms or systems operated by propellant charge energy for automatically opening the lock using a gas piston arranged concentrically around the barrel -> F41A5/20 - Gas operated mechanisms or systems operated by propellant charge energy for automatically opening the lock by direct action of gas pressure on bolt or locking elements -> F41A5/24 - Evacuating combustion gas from barrels -> F41A13/06 - Barrels, gun tubes, muzzle attachments, barrel mounting means -> F41A21/00
[F41A 5/28] Adjustable systems -- F41A 5/28 relates to adjustable mechanisms that employ the energy of a propellant charge to automatically open a weapon's lock, such as variable-position breech or trigger devices used in cannons and small arms. The entry covers systems that can be set to different positions or forces to control the lock-opening action.
[F41A 5/30] Gas- or recoil-operated, e.g. selection of gas- or recoil-operated systems -- The F41A 5/30 class covers mechanisms that use the energy of a propellant charge to automatically open the lock of firearms or artillery, including both gas-operated and recoil-operated systems. [F41A 5/32] Energy accumulator systems, i.e. systems for opening the breech-block by energy accumulated during barrel or gas piston recoil -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Implies a delayed or staged lock opening movement. The recoil or gas piston movement is accumulated, and the accumulated energy is used in a second stage to open the lock, after partial or full completion of the recoil/piston movement.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Recoil operated opening with moveable inertia weigth for storing energy -> F41A5/10
[F41A 5/34] with spring accumulators -- A mechanism for firearms or artillery that uses the energy of a propellant charge to automatically open the breech or lock, incorporating spring accumulators to store and release energy for the opening action. This functional feature applies to both small arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 5/36] with fluid accumulators -- This subclass concerns weapon lock-opening mechanisms that are powered by the energy of a propellant charge and employ fluid accumulators to store and release pressure for automatic operation.
[F41A 7/00] Auxiliary mechanisms for bringing the breech-block or bolt or the barrel to the starting position before automatic firing -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Mechanisms for automatically cycling a firearm independent of the propellant charge for bringing the moving components back into firing position enabling the choice of firing rate.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - External power or control systems for feeding and loading -> F41A9/50 - Shot velocity control -> F41A19/03

[F41A 7/08] Drives for externally-powered guns, i.e. drives for moving the breech-block or bolt by an external force during automatic firing -- F41A 7/08 covers mechanisms that use an external power source to move the breech-block or bolt of a gun during automatic firing, providing the drive that returns the action to the starting position. These externally-powered drives are employed in both small arms and larger ordnance to cycle the breech without relying on recoil energy.

[F41A 9/00] Feeding or loading of ammunition -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Devices an mechanisms for conveying the ammunition from its storage location or transport unit to the firing position.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Cartridge extractors or ejectors -> F41A15/00 - Adaptations for feeding or loading missiles from magazines in air guns -> F41B11/50  **Glossary:** - Feeding: Devices or mechanisms for sequentially bringing missiles from an external storage (magazine, belt, box) into the gun frame/casing/housing/receiver - Loading: Devices or mechanisms for sequentially bringing missiles into the firing chamber - Ramming: Devices or mechanisms for forcing a projectile into the constriciting area of the chamber or the beginning of the barrel itself

[F41A 9/03] using screw or rotary-spiral conveyors -- F41A 9/03 covers mechanisms that employ screw or rotary-spiral conveyors to move ammunition within weapons systems. [F41A 9/06] using cyclically moving conveyors, i.e. conveyors having ammunition pusher or carrier elements which are emptied or disengaged from the ammunition during the return stroke -- F41A 9/06 pertains to ammunition-feeding devices that use cyclically moving conveyors equipped with pusher or carrier elements which release the rounds during the return stroke, a mechanism employed in loading systems for both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 9/07] Reciprocating conveyors, i.e. conveyors pushing a plurality of ammunition during the feeding stroke -- F41A 9/07 covers reciprocating ammunition conveyors that move multiple rounds during the loading stroke of small arms or ordnance. [F41A 9/09] Movable ammunition carriers or loading trays, e.g. for feeding from magazines -- F41A 9/09 covers movable ammunition carriers or loading trays that transfer rounds from magazines to a weapon, including any device that can be shifted or repositioned to feed ammunition during loading or firing operations.

[F41A 9/16] parallel to the barrel axis -- F41A 9/16 covers ammunition-feeding or loading arrangements in weapons where the feed path runs parallel to the barrel axis, such as cartridge or shell feed strips, belts, or magazines aligned lengthwise with the gun, facilitating straight-line insertion into the chamber.

[F41A 9/20] sliding, e.g. reciprocating -- It refers to sliding or reciprocating feeding or loading mechanisms for small-arms and artillery, where ammunition is advanced by a linear motion of a component such as a bolt, carrier or strip. [F41A 9/21] in a vertical direction -- F41A 9/21 designates ammunition feeding or loading arrangements where the cartridge or projectile is moved vertically into the chamber of a small-arm, cannon or other ordnance. [F41A 9/37] Feeding two or more kinds of ammunition to the same gun -- The F41A 9/37 class covers mechanisms that enable a single gun-whether a small-arm or a larger ordnance piece-to receive two or more different types of ammunition through one feeding system. [F41A 9/39] Ramming arrangements -- F41A 9/39 pertains to ramming arrangements for feeding or loading ammunition in weapons, including small arms and larger ordnance. [F41A 9/41] pushing unbelted ammunition from a box magazine on the gun frame into the cartridge chamber -- The F41A 9/41 subclass covers mechanisms that push unbelted ammunition from a box magazine mounted on the gun's frame directly into the cartridge chamber, describing the pushing action used for loading in small-arms and ordnance rather than strip-feed or belt-feed methods.
[F41A 9/42] Rammers separate from breech-block -- F41A 9/42 relates to firearms or artillery where the rammer that seats the round is mounted separate from the breech-block, allowing the rammer to operate independently of the breech mechanism and improving loading efficiency.
[F41A 9/43] Chain rammers -- Chain rammers are loading mechanisms that use a moving chain to push ammunition into the breech of cannons or similar large firearms. The reciprocating chain feeds shells or cartridges, allowing faster, more reliable loading for artillery and related weapons.
[F41A 9/44] Fluid-operated piston rammers -- F41A 9/44 covers fluid-operated piston rammers employed in the feeding or loading of ammunition for small arms and larger ordnance such as cannons. [F41A 9/45] the cartridge chamber or the barrel as a whole being tiltable between a loading and a firing position -- F41A 9/45 classifies firearms or artillery in which the cartridge chamber or the barrel is hinged so it can tilt from a loading position to a firing alignment, facilitating the insertion of ammunition and then returning to fire. This mechanism is a specific feeding-or-loading feature common to both small-arms and larger ordnance.
[F41A 9/46] the cartridge chamber being formed by two complementary elements, movable one relative to the other for loading -- F41A 9/46 classifies weapon loading systems where the cartridge chamber consists of two complementary elements - a movable one relative to the other - to receive ammunition. [F41A 9/47] using forwardly-sliding barrels or barrel parts for loading -- F41A 9/47 covers mechanisms that use forward-sliding barrels or barrel components to load ammunition into firearms or artillery. [F41A 9/48] Loading by gravitational force -- F41A 9/48 pertains to weapon systems that rely on gravity to move ammunition into the loading position, such as drop-feed magazines, hoppers or inclined chambers where rounds fall into the breech or chamber without powered assistance.
[F41A 9/49] Internally-powered drives, i.e. operated by propellant charge energy, e.g. couplings, clutches, energy accumulators -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Mechanisms wherein the propellant charge energy is used for particular ammunition loading and feeding.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Mechanisms or systems operated by propellant charge energy for automatically opening the lock -> F41A5/00

[F41A 9/53] Charged-condition indicators, i.e. indicating the presence of a cartridge in the cartridge chamber -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Magazines having means for indicating the number of cartridges left in the magazine, e.g. last-round indicators -> F41A9/62 - Last round safeties -> F41A17/40
[F41A 9/54] Cartridge guides, stops or positioners, e.g. for cartridge extraction -- F41A 9/54 refers to devices that guide, stop or position cartridges within a firearm or artillery piece, facilitating reliable ammunition feeding and extraction. [F41A 9/55] Fixed guiding means, mounted on, or near, the cartridge chamber -- F41A 9/55 covers fixed guiding devices that are attached to or positioned close to the cartridge chamber to direct ammunition during loading or feeding.
