[F41J 9/18] Traps or throwing-apparatus therefor -- The F41J 9/18 code designates mechanical devices that capture or launch moving targets, such as traps and throwing-apparatus used for weapon testing or training. It covers systems intended to simulate or handle targets that are in motion when engaged.
[F41J 9/20] with spring-operated throwing arm -- F41J 9/20 classifies weapons designed to engage moving targets that employ a spring-operated throwing arm as the primary launching mechanism. It falls under the Mechanical Engineering hierarchy, within the Weapons and Targets categories, and specifies devices where the projectile is propelled by a spring-driven arm rather than explosive or pneumatic force.
[F41J 9/22] cocked by manual action -- The F41J 9/22 subclass deals with moving targets that must be cocked by a manual action, meaning the target is reset or prepared for the next shot using a hand-operated mechanism. It falls under mechanical engineering, weapons and targets, specifically addressing devices where the moving target is reset by the shooter or operator.
[F41J 9/24] cocked by electromechanical means -- F41J 9/24 covers systems for cocking moving targets by using electromechanical devices, such as motor-driven or solenoid mechanisms that reset the target after it has been hit. The classification falls under mechanical engineering weapons, specifically targets that move when engaged, and relates to automated re-arming methods.
[F41J 9/26] operated by fluid means -- A moving target that is propelled or repositioned by a fluid such as air, gas, steam or water falls under CPC class F41J 9/26. In the CPC hierarchy it lies in Mechanical Engineering to Weapons to Targets to Moving targets, and the subclass specifies operation by fluid means for training or testing of weapons.
[F41J 9/28] operated by manual action -- This CPC subclass covers weapon systems intended to engage targets that are moving when fired at, where the weapon's operation, aiming or propulsion is provided by manual human effort rather than automatic or powered mechanisms. It is nested under mechanical engineering → weapons → targets → moving targets.
[F41J 9/30] characterised by using a magazine of targets -- F41J 9/30 concerns weapon systems that employ a magazine of moving targets, delivering a series of targets that travel when fired at for continuous shooting practice. It falls under mechanical engineering of weapons and target apparatus.
[F41J 9/32] characterised by means for obviating the anticipation of the flight path -- F41J 9/32 classifies weapon-target technologies for moving targets that incorporate features to remove the need to predict the projectile's flight path. Such means may include real-time tracking, adaptive aiming or guidance systems that adjust continuously, ensuring the target is engaged without prior anticipation of its trajectory.
[F42] AMMUNITION -- F42 covers the design, manufacture and use of ammunition and related components such as cartridges, shells, missiles, propellants, primers and safety devices. It includes methods for loading, assembling, firing and controlling projectiles, as well as testing and performance evaluation. The class addresses both small arms and larger ballistic systems.
[F42B] EXPLOSIVE CHARGES, e.g. FOR BLASTING, FIREWORKS, AMMUNITION -- **Definition:** This place covers:  This subclass covers all mechanical and materials aspects of ammunition of all calibres, explosive charges, fireworks, non-firearm projectiles/missiles.  It also covers the aspects of manufacturing, destructions, dismantling, testing and packaging of ammunition.  It further covers accessories for use with ammunition not elsewhere classified.  The classification is generally only based on structural and functional aspects, irrespective of caliber scaling/sizing.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Chemical aspects of explosives, explosive compositions -> C06B - Fuzes -> F42C - Blasting -> F42D  **Glossary:** - Primer: effects the first explosive step in the sequence of explosion - Percussion cap: means a primer which is struck to explode - Igniter: effects the first spark-producing or heat-producing step but may not be explosive - Firing-means or initiator (used respectively in the arts of weaponry and blasting): means a device acting directly on the primer, which device may or may not form part of the fuze - Detonator or detonator charge: means a charge used to amplify the explosion of the primer - Fuze: means an assembly or mechanism which incorporates safety and arming means in order that the explosion can only take place under certain conditions; this assembly or mechanism determines also the moment (instantaneous or delayed) or the manner, e.g. impact, proximity, hydrostatic pressure, of the firing; - Ammunition: covers propulsive charge and projectile whether or not forming a single body, unless otherwise made clear - Projectile, missile or projectile or missile: means any body which is projected or propelled; - Guided missile: means projectile or missile which is guided during at least part of its trajectory; - Rocket: means projectile or missile which is self-propelled, during at least part of its trajectory, by a rocket engine, i.e. by a jet-propulsion engine carrying both fuel and oxidant therefor - Fuse or fuse cord: means a continuous train of explosive enclosed in a usually flexible cord or cable for setting-off an explosive charge in the art of blasting.
[F42B 1/00] Explosive charges characterised by form or shape but not dependent on shape of container -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Configuration, shape, manufacturing processes of explosive charges per se.  Shaped charges forming explosively formed projectiles or jets.
[F42B 10/00] Means for influencing, e.g. improving, the aerodynamic properties of projectiles or missiles -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Mechanical means for influencing, temporarily or constantly the aerodynamic properties of a projectile or missile or otherwise change its trajectory, i.e. the means physically influencing the projectile or missile to change behaviour. The influence being either the change of trajectory of the projectile or missile by intentional steering through remote control or on board control, the steering being effected with flight surfaces, thrust vector control or centre of gravity displacement. Alternatively the influence being the range increasing or the projectile or missile with aerodynamic means such as streamlined projectiles or lift inducing flight surfaces. Alternatively the influence could be the range reducing of the projectiles with aerodynamic spoilers or similar means. Alternatively the influence could be the fall retarding means, like parachutes or rotochutes, either with dropped warheads or in the final descent of the parabolic flight of a projectile.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Projectiles or missiles specially adapted for projection without use of explosive or combustible propellant charge, e.g. for blow guns, bows or crossbows, hand-held spring or air guns -> F42B6/00
[F42B 10/02] Stabilising arrangements -- F42B 10/02 covers stabilising arrangements for projectiles, missiles or explosive charges, i.e., devices or structural features that keep the item on a steady flight path or maintain its orientation during travel, such as fins, spin-stabilisers, gyroscopic assemblies or other aerodynamic control elements.
[F42B 10/025] using giratory or oscillating masses for stabilising projectile trajectory -- F42B 10/025 covers devices that employ rotating or oscillating masses to stabilize the flight path of projectiles or missiles, thereby enhancing their aerodynamic performance. Such mechanisms are used in ammunition and explosive charges to control trajectory and improve accuracy.
[F42B 10/04] using fixed fins -- F42B 10/04 covers the use of fixed fins on projectiles or missiles to modify their aerodynamic behavior, improving stability and flight performance.
[F42B 10/06] Tail fins -- F42B 10/06 covers tail fins, which are aerodynamic control surfaces fitted to projectiles or missiles to enhance stability and steerability by influencing airflow. The fins may be fixed or adjustable and are used to improve flight accuracy and range of ammunition, blasting charges, or fireworks. This subclass falls under means for influencing the aerodynamic properties of projectiles within the mechanical engineering ammunition section.
[F42B 10/08] Flechette-type projectiles -- Flechette-type projectiles are slender, dart-like munitions engineered for high-velocity penetration, and the classification covers their shape, stabilizing fins, tail designs and other aerodynamic enhancements that improve flight stability, range and impact performance.
[F42B 10/10] the fins being formed in the barrel by deformation or the projectile body -- F42B 10/10 relates to ammunition where the aerodynamic fins or the projectile body are produced by deformation inside the gun barrel rather than by separate attachment. It covers designs that generate stabilizing surfaces during firing to improve flight behavior.
[F42B 10/12] using fins longitudinally-slidable with respect to the projectile or missile -- This CPC subclass, F42B 10/12, covers projectile or missile designs in which aerodynamic control fins are mounted so they can slide forward or backward along the body. The movable fins are intended to adjust flight stability, trajectory or range during flight. It falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive charges.
[F42B 10/14] using fins spread or deployed after launch, e.g. after leaving the barrel -- F42B 10/14 covers devices that deploy or spread fins on a projectile after it has left the barrel to modify its flight characteristics. The fins are activated post-launch to improve stability, steerability or aerodynamic performance of missiles, shells or other ammunition. This method is classified under means for influencing the aerodynamic properties of projectiles.
[F42B 10/143] Lattice or grid fins -- F42B 10/143 covers lattice or grid fins-thin-walled, lattice-structured control surfaces attached to projectiles or missiles to modify their aerodynamic behavior, improve stability or maneuverability, and enhance flight performance. This subclass falls under mechanical engineering for ammunition and explosive charges, focusing on means for influencing projectile aerodynamics.
[F42B 10/146] Fabric fins, i.e. fins comprising at least one spar and a fin cover made of flexible sheet material -- Fabric fins are lightweight aerodynamic control surfaces for projectiles or missiles, comprising a flexible sheet that forms the fin cover and at least one spar to provide stiffness. This arrangement improves stability and flight performance of ammunition and explosive-charged devices.
[F42B 10/16] Wrap-around fins -- F42B 10/16 covers wrap-around fins that are attached to projectiles or missiles to modify their aerodynamic characteristics, such as stability and maneuverability, by extending around the body. These fins are used in ammunition and explosive charge applications to improve flight performance.
[F42B 10/18] using a longitudinally slidable support member -- The CPC entry F42B 10/18 covers devices that use a longitudinally slidable support member to adjust or enhance the aerodynamic behaviour of projectiles, missiles or explosive charges, typically to improve stability, flight path or propulsive efficiency. It falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive-charge technologies for influencing projectile aerodynamics.
[F42B 1/02] Shaped or hollow charges -- **Definition:** This place covers:  A hollow charge is a container filled with an explosive at least one side of the explosive having a concave hollow, which collapses upon detonation, the detonation of the explosive being initiated at the side opposite of the hollow. The hollow may be conical in shape to produce a jet or hemi-spherical to produce a projectile. The hollow is usually delimited by a liner.  A more generic shaped charge may have a convex active surface with an optional liner.  **Glossary:** - Liner: thin metal layer, usually made of metal, disposed on the concave part of the hollow charge which is formed into a particle jet or an explosively formed projectile upon detonation of the charge
[F42B 10/20] deployed by combustion gas pressure, or by pneumatic or hydraulic forces -- The CPC class F42B 10/20 covers means for deploying explosive charges by means of combustion-generated gas pressure, pneumatic pressure, or hydraulic force, aimed at improving the aerodynamic behaviour of projectiles or missiles. It falls under the hierarchy of mechanical engineering → ammunition → explosive charges → aerodynamic influence of projectiles.
[F42B 10/22] Projectiles of cannelured type -- F42B 10/22 classifies projectiles that feature one or more longitudinal grooves, or cannelures, cut into the body to modify airflow, increase stability or allow better engagement with sabot or rifling. These cannelured projectiles are designed to improve aerodynamic performance during flight.
[F42B 1/024] provided with embedded bodies of inert material -- F42B 1/024 covers explosive charges whose design is defined by their form or shape and that incorporate bodies of inert material embedded within the charge. This subclass falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition, and explosive-charge categories, focusing on shape-based characteristics independent of the container's geometry.
[F42B 10/24] with inclined grooves -- F42B 10/24 covers devices that improve the aerodynamic performance of projectiles or missiles by adding inclined grooves to their surfaces. The grooves are designed to alter airflow, reduce drag and increase stability and range.
[F42B 10/26] using spin -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Stabilising arrangements using fixed fins -> F42B10/04 - Stabilising arrangements using fins longitudinally-slidable with respect to the projectile or missile -> F42B10/12 - Stabilising arrangements using fins spread or deployed after launch, e.g. after leaving the barrel -> F42B10/14 - Projectiles of cannelured type with inclined grooves -> F42B10/24 - Driving bands; Rotating bands -> F42B14/02 - Means for imparting spin to the rocket before launching -> F41F3/048
[F42B 1/028] characterised by the form of the liner -- This subclass covers explosive charges such as shaped charges used in blasting or munitions where the performance is defined by the geometry of the metal liner that forms the jet rather than by the shape of the surrounding casing. It includes designs with conical, hemispherical or other liner shapes that control jet formation.
[F42B 10/28] induced by gas action -- F42B 10/28 covers methods that use the action of gases to alter the aerodynamic behavior of projectiles or missiles, such as gas-driven control surfaces, thrust-vectoring jets, or gas-based flow control devices that improve stability, lift or drag characteristics during flight. This subclass sits within mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive-charge technologies focused on aerodynamic influence.
[F42B 10/30] using rocket motor nozzles -- This subclass covers the use of rocket motor nozzles to modify thrust vector, exhaust flow, or other characteristics that affect flight stability, guidance, or range of projectiles and missiles.
[F42B 1/032] characterised by the material of the liner -- The subclass F42B 1/032 covers explosive charges whose liners are distinguished by the material they are made of rather than by shape. It belongs to the mechanical engineering field for ammunition and other explosive charges.
[F42B 10/32] Range-reducing or range-increasing arrangements -- F42B 10/32 covers devices or methods that alter the flight distance of projectiles or missiles, either shortening or extending their range. This includes aerodynamic shaping, weight adjustments, propellant modifications or launch-angle controls designed to influence how far the munition travels. It falls under mechanical engineering for explosive charges and projectile performance.
[F42B 10/34] Tubular projectiles -- F42B 10/34 covers tubular projectiles whose shape or internal features are adapted to influence their aerodynamic behavior, such as improving stability, range, or flight characteristics. It includes design elements like fins, nose cones, or internal liners that modify airflow around the projectile. The class is used for inventions that enhance the performance of bullets, shells or missiles by altering their aerodynamic properties.
[F42B 1/036] Manufacturing processes therefor -- F42B 1/036 covers manufacturing techniques for explosive charges whose classification depends on their intrinsic shape rather than the shape of the surrounding container. It includes processes such as shaping, molding, pressing, cutting or assembling the charge material to achieve the required geometry for blasting, fireworks or ammunition applications.
[F42B 10/36] Ring-foil projectiles -- F42B 10/36 covers ring-foil projectiles, which incorporate one or more thin metal rings or foils attached to the projectile body to modify airflow and improve stability, lift, or drag characteristics during flight. The design is used to enhance aerodynamic performance of missiles, shells, or fireworks, often by generating controlled vortex shedding or altering pressure distribution.
[F42B 10/38] Range-increasing arrangements -- F42B 10/38 covers devices or methods that increase the range of projectiles or missiles by modifying their aerodynamic characteristics. This includes aerodynamic shaping, launch boosters, and other arrangements aimed at extending flight distance for ammunition and explosive charges.
[F42B 1/04] Detonator charges not forming part of the fuze -- The subclass F42B 1/04 defines detonator charges that are not integrated into the fuze itself, covering separate initiating charges used to ignite the main explosive load. It falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive-charge categories that are classified by form rather than the shape of their container.
[F42B 10/40] with combustion of a slow-burning charge, e.g. fumers, base-bleed projectiles -- F42B 10/40 is a CPC subclass that addresses means for influencing the aerodynamic performance of projectiles or missiles by using the combustion of a slow-burning charge, such as fumers or base-bleed devices. It is situated under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive-charge categories.
[F42B 10/42] Streamlined projectiles -- F42B 10/42 covers designs and features that improve the aerodynamic shape of projectiles or missiles, such as nose cones, fins or surface treatments that reduce drag and enhance stability in flight. It falls under the ammunition subclass dealing with explosive charges and methods for influencing projectile flight characteristics.
[F42B 10/44] Boat-tails specially adapted for drag reduction -- This subclass covers aerodynamic devices attached to the rear of projectiles-boat-tails-that are shaped or configured to lower drag and improve flight stability. It includes designs that modify airflow to reduce deceleration and extend range for shells, missiles or similar munitions.
[F42B 10/46] Streamlined nose cones -- **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Protruding target distance or stand-off members therefor, e.g. slidably mounted -> F42B12/105
[F42B 10/48] Range-reducing, destabilising or braking arrangements, -- F42B 10/48 covers devices that intentionally reduce the range, destabilise the flight or provide braking for projectiles or missiles, such as aerodynamic spoilers, drag-inducing fittings or other mechanisms designed to modify trajectory.
[F42B 10/50] Brake flaps -- Brake flaps are aerodynamic control surfaces fitted to projectiles or missiles that deploy to increase drag and reduce speed, allowing precise range or trajectory adjustment. They are typically used on artillery shells, rockets or guided munitions to improve flight stability and permit rapid braking after launch. This technology falls under the CPC class F42B 10/50 for means influencing projectile aerodynamics.
[F42B 10/52] Nose cones -- F42B 10/52 covers nose cones, the forward-facing structures fitted to projectiles, missiles or rockets to shape airflow, reduce drag and enhance stability or thermal protection. These components are used to improve the aerodynamic performance of explosive charges and related ammunition.
[F42B 10/54] Spin braking means -- F42B 10/54 covers spin braking means, devices that reduce or control the spin of a projectile to improve its aerodynamic performance. This subclass falls under mechanical engineering ammunition and explosive charges, specifically methods for influencing the flight characteristics of missiles or shells.
[F42B 10/56] of parachute type -- F42B 10/56 covers parachute-type devices employed to modify the aerodynamic performance of projectiles or missiles, for example by decelerating, stabilizing, or controlling their flight after launch. These means are used in ammunition and explosive charge applications to influence trajectory and impact behavior.
[F42B 10/58] of rotochute type -- It covers rotochute-type devices that are attached to projectiles or missiles to modify their aerodynamic behavior, typically by deploying a rotating parachute-like structure to stabilize or control the trajectory after launch. The classification falls under means for influencing the aerodynamic properties of ammunition.
[F42B 10/60] Steering arrangements -- F42B 10/60 covers steering arrangements for projectiles or missiles, including devices and mechanisms that adjust flight path to improve aerodynamic performance. It includes control surfaces, movable fins, thrust vectoring systems, and related means for influencing trajectory of explosive charges such as shells, rockets, or fireworks.
[F42B 10/62] Steering by movement of flight surfaces -- F42B 10/62 concerns methods and devices that steer projectiles or missiles by moving flight surfaces to alter their aerodynamic properties.
[F42B 10/64] of fins -- F42B 10/64 pertains to the use of fins on projectiles or missiles to alter their aerodynamic performance. It addresses the design, placement and shaping of fins that improve flight stability, control trajectory or increase lift, thereby influencing the behavior of ammunition and explosive charges during flight.
[F42B 10/66] Steering by varying intensity or direction of thrust -- The F42B 10/66 class covers techniques for steering projectiles or missiles by changing the intensity or direction of their thrust. It includes systems that vary propulsion during flight to alter trajectory and improve aerodynamic performance.
[F42B 10/661] using several transversally acting rocket motors, each motor containing an individual propellant charge, e.g. solid charge -- The entry covers projectile or missile systems that employ multiple rocket motors positioned transversely, each motor having its own propellant charge-usually solid-to modify thrust distribution and improve the aerodynamic performance of the ammunition.
[F42B 10/663] using a plurality of transversally acting auxiliary nozzles, which are opened or closed by valves -- This subclass covers projectile or missile systems that use several side-acting auxiliary nozzles, each opened or closed by valves, to modify airflow and thrust in order to improve aerodynamic performance, stability or trajectory during flight.
[F42B 10/665] characterised by using a nozzle provided with at least a deflector mounted within the nozzle -- The F42B 10/665 entry covers ammunition or explosive devices that improve projectile or missile aerodynamics by employing a nozzle that contains at least one deflector mounted inside the nozzle, thereby influencing the flow of gases or combustion products for better flight performance.
[F42B 10/666] characterised by using a nozzle rotatable about an axis transverse to the axis of the projectile -- This subclass relates to projectiles or missiles that incorporate a nozzle capable of rotating around an axis perpendicular to the projectile's longitudinal axis, allowing adjustment of thrust direction to improve aerodynamic performance or trajectory control.
[F42B 10/668] Injection of a fluid, e.g. a propellant, into the gas shear in a nozzle or in the boundary layer at the outer surface of a missile, e.g. to create a shock wave in a supersonic flow -- This CPC subclass covers methods of injecting a fluid, such as a propellant, into the gas shear in a nozzle or into the boundary layer on the outer surface of a missile to alter aerodynamic properties, for example to create shock waves in supersonic flow.
[F42B 12/00] Projectiles, missiles or mines characterised by the warhead, the intended effect, or the material -- **Definition:** This place covers:  The warheads of self-propelled missiles and rockets as well as projectiles which are not self propelled ( F42B15/00 ) are classified according to two major aspects, i.e. their intended effect ( F42B12/02 - F42B12/72 ) and the material of the projectile ( F42B12/74 - F42B12/82 ). In this whole main group, the ammunition is classified according to functional features.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Projectiles or missiles specially adapted for projection without use of explosive or combustible propellant charge, e.g. for blow guns, bows or crossbows, hand-held spring or air guns -> F42B6/00 - Means for influencing, e.g. improving, the aerodynamic properties of projectiles or missiles; Arrangements on projectiles or missiles for stabilising, steering, range-reducing, range-increasing or fall-retarding -> F42B10/00 - Projectiles or missiles characterised by arrangements for guiding or sealing them inside barrels, or for lubricating or cleaning barrels -> F42B14/00
[F42B 12/02] characterised by the warhead or the intended effect -- F42B 12/02 groups projectiles, missiles or mines that are defined primarily by the type of warhead they carry or the effect they are designed to produce, such as penetration, fragmentation, incendiary or blast impact. The subclass is used to classify weapons where the warhead's nature or intended outcome is the principal distinguishing feature.
[F42B 12/04] of armour-piercing type -- F42B 12/04 covers projectiles, missiles or mines whose warhead is of an armor-piercing type, intended to penetrate protective armor or hardened targets. The classification falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition, explosive charges and specifies the warhead design and intended effect.
[F42B 12/06] with hard or heavy core -- F42B 12/06 covers projectiles, missiles or mines whose warhead contains a hard or heavy core, such as armor-piercing or penetrator elements. The classification applies to designs where the core material or density is a key functional feature for achieving a specified effect on the target. It falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition, explosive charges, and warhead-characterised weapons.
[F42B 12/08] with armour-piercing caps -- This subclass covers projectiles, missiles or mines that incorporate armor-piercing caps, such as steel or tungsten nose caps designed to improve penetration of armor or hardened targets. The classification is used for ammunition where the warhead includes a dedicated AP cap.
[F42B 12/10] with shaped or hollow charge -- F42B 12/10 covers projectiles, missiles or mines that employ a shaped or hollow charge to concentrate explosive energy for penetration or a specific effect. It sits within Mechanical Engineering → Ammunition → Explosive charges, focusing on warhead designs that generate a high-velocity jet to defeat armor or create a controlled blast.
[F42B 12/105] Protruding target distance or stand-off members therefor, e.g. slidably mounted  fuze aspects  F42C1/14 -- F42B 12/105 concerns devices that provide a measured stand-off distance between a projectile's warhead and the target, such as protruding members or slidably mounted fuze components that allow the charge to detonate at a preset separation.
[F42B 12/12] rotatably mounted with respect to missile housing -- The F42B 12/12 subclass relates to missiles, projectiles or mines whose warhead is attached so it can rotate with respect to the missile housing, enabling the warhead to be oriented or aiming-adjusted during flight or after launch. This design feature is covered under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive-charge classifications.
[F42B 12/14] the symmetry axis of the hollow charge forming an angle with the longitudinal axis of the projectile -- The code F42B 12/14 covers projectiles whose hollow-charge warhead is oriented so that its symmetry axis forms a specific angle with the projectile's longitudinal axis. It addresses design features that affect the distribution of jet and blast effects in missiles, bombs or mines.
[F42B 12/16] in combination with an additional projectile or charge, acting successively on the target -- F42B 12/16 refers to ammunition where a primary projectile is paired with a secondary projectile or charge that engages the target in a successive manner, such as tandem warheads or multi-stage explosive devices designed to defeat layered defenses or achieve a combined effect.
[F42B 12/18] Hollow charges in tandem arrangement -- The subclass F42B 12/18 covers projectile warheads that use two or more hollow (shaped) charges arranged in tandem, where a precursor charge initiates before a main charge to enhance penetration or effect. It applies to ammunition and explosive devices whose performance relies on the sequential detonation of these charges.
[F42B 12/20] of high-explosive type -- F42B 12/20 covers projectiles, missiles or mines whose warhead is of the high-explosive type, focusing on the composition, design and performance of the explosive charge that produces a blast effect. It is used to classify items where the primary destructive mechanism is a high-explosive material rather than fragmentation, incendiary or other effects. This subclass falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive charges in the CPC system.
[F42B 12/201] characterised by target class -- F42B 12/201 classifies projectiles, missiles or mines whose warhead is defined by the class of target they are intended to engage, such as personnel, armored vehicles, aircraft or structures. It falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive charges, focusing on target-based warhead characterization.
[F42B 12/202] for attacking land area or area targets, e.g. airburst -- F42B 12/202 classifies projectiles, missiles or mines whose warheads are intended to attack land areas or area targets, often using an airburst detonation to affect a wide zone.
[F42B 12/204] for attacking structures, e.g. specific buildings or fortifications, ships or vehicles -- F42B 12/204 defines projectiles, missiles or mines whose warhead is intended to attack and damage structures such as buildings, fortifications, ships or vehicles. The subclass falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition, explosive charges and classifies weapons based on their destructive effect on fixed or mobile structures.
[F42B 12/205] for attacking aerial targets -- This subclass classifies warheads specifically designed to engage aerial targets such as aircraft, helicopters, drones or missiles, covering projectiles, missiles or mines whose explosive charge or effect is intended for use against objects in flight. It sits under mechanical engineering → ammunition → explosive charges, focusing on the warhead's intended effect on air-borne targets.
[F42B 12/207] characterised by the explosive material or the construction of the high explosive warhead, e.g. insensitive ammunition -- This subclass covers projectiles, missiles or mines whose warhead is defined by the type of high-explosive material or by its construction, including designs using insensitive or specially formulated explosives. It is situated within the mechanical-engineering hierarchy under ammunition and explosive charges.
[F42B 12/208] characterised by a plurality of charges within a single high explosive warhead -- F42B 12/208 refers to warheads in ammunition where a single high-explosive casing contains several separate charges. The classification covers designs in which multiple explosive elements are arranged to achieve a specific blast effect or timing within one projectile.
[F42B 12/22] with fragmentation-hull construction -- F42B 12/22 covers projectiles, missiles or mines whose warhead incorporates a fragmentation-hull construction, wherein the outer casing is designed to break into fragments upon detonation to achieve a specific effect such as shrapnel dispersal. It belongs to the CPC class for ammunition and explosive charges.
[F42B 12/24] with grooves, recesses or other wall weakenings -- F42B 12/24 covers projectiles, missiles or mines whose warhead walls contain grooves, recesses or other intentional weakenings to influence fragmentation, shape-charge behavior or controlled break-up during detonation. It falls under the ammunition subclass of explosive charges within mechanical engineering. This feature is used to achieve specific damage effects or to manage the blast pattern.
[F42B 12/26] the projectile wall being formed by a spirally-wound element -- F42B 12/26 relates to ammunition whose projectile wall is made from a spirally-wound element, such as a helical metal or composite jacket that forms the outer casing of a missile, shell or mine. The classification covers the design, manufacturing and structural features of these spiral-wound walls that influence the warhead's effect, durability or fragmentation.
[F42B 12/28] the projectile wall being built from annular elements -- F42B 12/28 relates to projectiles whose structural wall is constructed from annular elements, such as rings or doughnut-shaped sections, forming a modular or segmented warhead body.
[F42B 12/30] Continuous-rod warheads -- Continuous-rod warheads are explosive projectiles that contain a mesh or series of metal rods arranged in a cylindrical shape; when the charge detonates, the rods are driven outward and expand into a high-velocity ring that slices through targets. This design increases the lethal radius by producing a large cutting effect, making it useful for anti-aircraft, anti-missile and other defensive applications.
[F42B 12/32] the hull or case comprising a plurality of discrete bodies, e.g. steel balls, embedded therein -- F42B 12/32 covers projectiles whose hull or case is built from a matrix that incorporates a plurality of separate bodies, such as steel balls, to create internal fragmentation or other functional effects. The classification applies to warheads where these embedded particles are an integral part of the explosive charge design.
[F42B 12/34] expanding before or on impact, i.e. of dumdum or mushroom type -- F42B 12/34 covers projectiles whose warhead expands on or just before impact, such as dumdum or mushroom-type designs, to increase damage or produce a specific effect. It falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition, explosive charges and warhead-characterized missiles or mines.
[F42B 12/36] for dispensing materials -- F42B 12/36 covers devices or methods for dispensing materials from projectiles, missiles or mines, such as releasing powders, liquids or other substances at the point of impact or during flight. The classification addresses mechanisms that control timing, quantity and distribution of the payload to achieve a specific warhead effect.

[F42B 12/56] for dispensing discrete solid bodies -- F42B 12/56 covers devices or methods that dispense individual solid bodies from ammunition or explosive charges, such as releasing separate projectiles, warheads or other discrete solid elements during launch or detonation. [F42B 12/58] Cluster or cargo ammunition, i.e. projectiles containing one or more submissiles -- F42B 12/58 relates to cluster or cargo ammunition, i.e. projectiles that carry and release one or more sub-munitions during flight or on impact. [F42B 12/625] a single submissile arranged in a carrier missile for being launched or accelerated coaxially; Coaxial tandem arrangement of missiles which are active in the target one after the other  with shaped or hollow charges  F42B12/16 -- F42B 12/625 classifies weapons in which a single sub-munition is housed inside a carrier missile and launched or accelerated along the same axis as the carrier. [F42B 12/80] Coatings -- F42B 12/80 pertains to the application of protective or functional coatings on projectiles, missiles, mines or other warheads. [F42B 12/82] reducing friction -- F42B 12/82 concerns methods or devices that lower friction in the propulsion or flight of warheads, missiles, mines or other explosive projectiles, covering surface treatments, lubricants, material choices or geometric modifications that improve performance and reliability of such ammunition.
[F42B 14/00] Projectiles or missiles characterised by arrangements for guiding or sealing them inside barrels, or for lubricating or cleaning barrels -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Includes the means for guiding the projectiles with nominal calibre inside the barrel with one ore more malleable driving bands around the periphery of the projectile.  Also includes obturators for sealing the projectile so that no propellant gas leaks around the projectile.  Further includes coatings on projectiles with provisions for lubricating, i.e. reducing the friction between the barrel and the projectile.  Also includes all the sub-calibre projectiles, in particular the means for guiding them inside the normal calibre barrel, such as obturator plates and sabots.
[F42B 14/02] Driving bands -- F42B 14/02 Driving bands are components fitted around projectiles that engage the barrel's rifling or bore to create a seal, transmit spin and reduce friction, often also serving to lubricate or protect the barrel during firing. This entry falls under mechanical engineering for ammunition, specifically explosive charges and projectile guidance or sealing arrangements.
[F42B 14/04] Lubrication means in missiles -- F42B 14/04 refers to lubrication means incorporated in missiles or projectile barrels to reduce friction, prevent seizing and aid cleaning during launch. The class includes oil or grease reservoirs, applicator devices and coating systems placed within the barrel or launch tube. It is a sub-category of mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive-charge projectile guidance or barrel-sealing arrangements.
[F42B 14/06] Sub-calibre projectiles having sabots -- Sub-calibre projectiles equipped with sabots are smaller-diameter projectiles that use a detachable carrier to fit snugly inside a larger-calibre barrel, ensuring proper guidance, sealing and barrel cleaning during firing. The sabot transmits the propellant force to the projectile and separates after exiting the muzzle, allowing the sub-calibre projectile to continue on its trajectory. This subclass addresses such sabot-based ammunition designs.
[F42B 14/061] Sabots for long rod fin stabilised kinetic energy projectiles, i.e. multisegment sabots attached midway on the projectile -- F42B 14/061 covers multisegment sabots that are mounted part-way along long-rod, fin-stabilised kinetic-energy projectiles to guide, seal and protect the barrel during launch. The sabots are designed to split or release after the projectile leaves the barrel, enhancing flight stability and minimizing barrel wear.
[F42B 14/062] characterised by contact surfaces between projectile and sabot -- F42B 14/062 covers projectiles or missiles whose design specifies the contact surfaces between the projectile and its sabot, detailing how these interfaces guide, seal, lubricate or clean the barrel during firing. It falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive-charge classifications related to barrel-fitting arrangements.
[F42B 14/064] Sabots enclosing the rear end of a kinetic energy projectile, i.e. having a closed disk shaped obturator base and petals extending forward from said base -- F42B 14/064 covers sabots that seal the rear of a kinetic-energy projectile, featuring a solid, disk-shaped obturator base with forward-extending petal sections. The design provides barrel guidance, sealing, and lubrication while the projectile exits the barrel, and is used in ammunition such as artillery shells and guided missiles.
[F42B 14/065] Sabots carrying several projectiles -- This subclass relates to sabots designed to hold two or more projectiles together for simultaneous discharge, providing guidance, sealing, lubrication or cleaning of the barrel during firing. The arrangement enables the projectiles to be released sequentially or together after leaving the barrel.
[F42B 14/067] Sealing aspects in sabots, e.g. sealing between individual segments of the sabots or sealing between the outer surface of the sabot and the inner surface of the barrel -- F42B 14/067 pertains to sealing aspects in sabots for projectiles, describing ways to seal gaps between individual sabot segments or between the sabot's outer surface and the barrel interior to maintain guidance and prevent gas leakage during firing.
[F42B 14/068] Sabots characterised by the material  F42B14/067  takes precedence
[F42B 14/08] Sabots filled with propulsive charges -- The subclass concerns sabots-support sleeves that enable a sub-caliber projectile to be fired from a larger barrel-when the sabot itself contains a propulsive charge used to launch the projectile. It relates to ammunition that integrates guiding, sealing or barrel-cleaning functions together with an internal propellant.
[F42B 15/00] Self-propelled projectiles or missiles, e.g. rockets -- **Definition:** This place covers:  This main group covers all the missiles and projectiles with their own propulsion system and/or guidance system. The rockets and missiles must have at least part of their trajectory in the air.  These self-propelled missiles main group is structured according to the following aspects:  Mechanical and integrated arrangements for guidance or control mounted on the missile.  Their application without warhead (measurement instruments carriers)  The medium they are designed for (air or water and air).  Functional features such as heat shields/cooling arrangements and multi-stage rocket connectors and/or disconnectors.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Means for influencing, e.g. improving, the aerodynamic properties of projectiles or missiles; Arrangements on projectiles or missiles for stabilising, steering, range-reducing, range-increasing or fall-retarding -> F42B10/00 - Stabilising of missiles -> F42B10/02 - Steering of missiles -> F42B10/66 - Projectiles, missiles or mines characterised by the warhead, the intended effect, or the material -> F42B12/00 - Projectiles or missiles characterised by arrangements for guiding or sealing them inside barrels, or for lubricating or cleaning barrels -> F42B14/00 - Rocket torpedo -> F42B17/00 - Marine torpedoes -> F42B19/00 - Electronic or logic guidandce and control of missiles -> F41G7/00
[F42B 15/01] Arrangements thereon for guidance or control -- It includes designs of components attached to rockets or other self-propelled projectiles that enable directional control, such as fins, canards, thrust-vectoring nozzles, or other steering mechanisms. The focus is on the physical arrangement of these guidance or control elements rather than on electronic or software aspects.
[F42B 15/04] using wire, e.g. for guiding ground-to-ground rockets -- F42B 15/04 relates to self-propelled projectiles or missiles that are guided by a wire, specifically covering the use of a wire for steering ground-to-ground rockets. This classification falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition, and explosive charges for blasting, fireworks and ammunition.
[F42B 15/08] for carrying measuring instruments; ; -- F42B 15/08 covers self-propelled projectiles or missiles that are equipped with a provision for carrying measuring instruments, such as sensors or data-recording devices, to monitor performance or environmental conditions during flight. This subgroup falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition, and explosive charges, specifically relating to rockets and similar missile types.
[F42B 15/10] Missiles having a trajectory only in the air -- F42B 15/10 designates air-only missiles, i.e., self-propelled projectiles whose entire flight path stays in the atmosphere from launch to impact, covering rockets and guided weapons that do not travel through water or underground.
[F42B 15/105] Air torpedoes, e.g. projectiles with or without propulsion, provided with supporting air foil surfaces -- F42B 15/105 covers air torpedoes, which are projectiles - propelled or not - that employ supporting air-foil surfaces to generate lift or stability during flight. The classification applies to self-propelled or unpowered weapons that function as missiles or rockets within the broader category of explosive charges.
[F42B 15/12] Intercontinental ballistic missiles -- F42B 15/12 covers intercontinental ballistic missiles, self-propelled projectiles designed for long-range delivery of explosive warheads across continents. The code classifies technical features of propulsion, guidance, and structural elements specific to ICBM systems within the broader category of ammunition and explosive charges.
[F42B 15/20] Missiles having a trajectory beginning below water surface -- F42B 15/20 covers missiles that are launched from beneath the water surface, describing their design and operation as self-propelled projectiles whose trajectory begins underwater, pertinent to underwater weaponry and related mechanical engineering fields.
[F42B 15/22] Missiles having a trajectory finishing below water surface -- F42B 15/22 covers missiles that are designed to complete their flight path beneath the water surface, including launch, guidance, and impact mechanisms for underwater targets. The class includes self-propelled projectiles such as torpedo-like rockets and submersible warheads used in naval or anti-ship applications.
[F42B 15/34] Protection against overheating or radiation, e.g. heat shields -- F42B 15/34 covers protective systems for self-propelled projectiles such as rockets, specifically heat shields or other measures that guard the missile against overheating or radiation damage during flight.
[F42B 15/36] Means for interconnecting rocket-motor and body section -- F42B 15/36 covers devices that join a rocket's propulsion unit to the vehicle or missile body, such as couplings, adapters or joint structures that securely transmit thrust while allowing assembly, disassembly or alignment of the motor and airframe.
[F42B 15/38] Ring-shaped explosive elements for the separation of rocket parts -- This classification pertains to ring-shaped explosive devices designed to separate rocket parts, such as stages or payload sections, during launch or operation. The rings are positioned around the components and detonate to produce a controlled fracture that frees the sections for subsequent flight phases.
[F42B 17/00] Rocket torpedoes, i.e. missiles provided with separate propulsion means for movement through air and through water -- **Definition:** This place covers:  A rocket torpedo is a special case of a self-propelled projectile or missile.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Projectiles, missiles or mines characterised by the warhead, the intended effect, or the material -> F42B12/00 - Marine torpedoes -> F42B19/00
[F42B 19/00] Marine torpedoes, e.g. launched by surface vessels or submarines -- **Definition:** This place covers:  All aspects relating to torpedo, i.e. self-propelled and/or guided missile with a trajectory mainly under water, they may be lauched from surface vessels or aircraft, however their auto-propulsion system is designed for underwater use.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Projectiles, missiles or mines characterised by the warhead, the intended effect, or the material -> F42B12/00
[F42B 19/005] Nose caps for torpedoes; Coupling torpedo-case parts together -- The F42B 19/005 subclass concerns nose caps fitted to marine torpedoes and the means of joining the separate sections of a torpedo's outer case. It covers designs that protect the torpedo front, improve hydrodynamic performance, and securely couple case components together.
[F42B 19/01] Steering control -- F42B 19/01 covers steering control mechanisms for marine torpedoes, including those launched from surface ships or submarines. The classification falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition, and explosive charges, focusing on devices that guide torpedo trajectory during operation.
[F42B 19/04] Depth control -- F42B 19/04 covers systems and methods for controlling the depth of marine torpedoes, including devices that adjust buoyancy, ballast, hydroplanes, or propulsion to maintain a preset underwater trajectory. The subclass is part of mechanical engineering for explosive charges used in torpedoes.
[F42B 19/06] Directional control -- F42B 19/06 covers systems that provide directional control for marine torpedoes, including steering, guidance and maneuvering devices used in torpedoes launched from surface vessels or submarines.
[F42B 19/08] with means for preventing rolling or pitching -- F42B 19/08 is a CPC subclass covering marine torpedoes that incorporate devices to prevent rolling or pitching, such as gyroscopes, fins, control surfaces or other stabilising mechanisms, ensuring a steady trajectory after launch. It lies within mechanical engineering → ammunition → explosive charges → marine torpedoes.
[F42B 19/10] remotely controlled, e.g. by sonic or radio control -- This classification covers marine torpedoes that are remote-controlled, for example by acoustic or radio signals, used in naval warfare or underwater demolition. It is indexed under CPC group F42B 19/10 and relates specifically to the control methods for such torpedoes.
[F42B 19/12] Propulsion specially adapted for torpedoes -- The CPC class F42B 19/12 relates to propulsion systems that are specially designed for marine torpedoes, including those launched from surface vessels or submarines. It is nested under mechanical engineering → ammunition → explosive charges, covering power-train solutions tailored to underwater weaponry.
[F42B 19/125] Torpedoes provided with drag-reducing means  projectiles with drag-reducing means  F42B10/38 -- F42B 19/125 covers torpedoes equipped with any drag-reducing features, such as streamlined hulls, surface coatings, or flow-control devices, aimed at improving speed and efficiency in water. The subclass falls within the CPC group for marine torpedoes under mechanical engineering and explosive charges.
[F42B 19/14] by compressed-gas motors -- F42B 19/14 refers to marine torpedoes that are propelled by compressed-gas motors, using stored high-pressure air or other gases to drive a propeller or piston system instead of combustion or electric power. It covers designs where the gas-driven mechanism provides thrust for torpedoes launched from surface ships or submarines.
[F42B 19/16] of cylinder type -- F42B 19/16 covers cylinder-type marine torpedoes, i.e., torpedoes of a cylindrical shape launched from surface vessels or submarines, including their design, construction, propulsion and fuze arrangements. This sub-group falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive charges for blasting, fireworks and armament.
[F42B 19/18] of turbine type -- F42B 19/18 covers marine torpedoes that use a turbine-driven propulsion system, typically launched from surface vessels or submarines. The classification pertains to turbine-type torpedoes within the broader category of explosive charges for naval use.
[F42B 19/20] characterised by the composition of propulsive gas -- F42B 19/20 designates torpedo propulsion systems where the propulsive gas composition is the defining feature. It falls under mechanical engineering - ammunition - explosive charges for marine torpedoes, covering inventions that specify the chemical makeup of the gases used to drive the torpedo.
[F42B 19/22] by internal-combustion engines -- F42B 19/22 covers marine torpedoes that are propelled using internal-combustion engines, including those launched from surface ships or submarines. The subclass addresses the design, operation and related systems of combustion-driven torpedoes within the broader category of explosive charges.
[F42B 19/24] by electric motors -- The CPC class F42B 19/24 covers marine torpedoes-propulsion devices launched from surface ships or submarines-that are driven by electric motors. It falls under Mechanical Engineering → Ammunition → Explosive Charges, specifying the electric-motor propulsion method for such underwater weapons.
[F42B 19/26] by jet propulsion -- F42B 19/26 covers marine torpedoes that are propelled by jet propulsion, using a high-velocity stream of water or gas expelled from the torpedo to generate thrust. It is a subclass in the CPC hierarchy under mechanical engineering, ammunition, explosive charges, specifically marine torpedoes launched from surface vessels or submarines.
[F42B 19/28] with means for avoiding visible wake -- The F42B 19/28 subclass covers marine torpedoes that incorporate devices or design features intended to suppress or eliminate the visible wake produced during launch or travel, such as wake-reducing propellers, bubble-generating systems, or low-profile hull shapes, thereby enhancing stealth and reducing detection risk.
[F42B 19/30] with timing control of propulsion -- This CPC subclass covers marine torpedoes whose propulsion system incorporates timing control to initiate or regulate thrust. It falls under mechanical engineering, ammunition, and explosive charges, specifically for torpedoes launched from surface ships or submarines.
[F42B 19/36] adapted to be used for exercise purposes, e.g. indicating position or course -- The subclass F42B 19/36 covers marine torpedoes designed for training or practice, where the weapon is modified to simulate position or course without a live warhead. These exercise torpedoes replicate the handling and performance of operational torpedoes while using inert payloads or reduced explosives for safety.
[F42B 19/38] with means for causing torpedoes to surface at end of run -- F42B 19/38 pertains to marine torpedoes that include a system to bring the weapon to the surface once its run is complete, such as buoyant devices or explosive charges that cause surfacing. The feature is designed for torpedoes launched from surface ships or submarines and facilitates recovery or secondary effects.
[F42B 19/40] by expelling liquid ballast -- F42B 19/40 covers torpedoes that control their depth or trajectory by expelling liquid ballast. The method uses a fluid-filled tank that is released to reduce weight and change buoyancy, allowing the weapon to dive or rise without mechanical fins. This technique is applied to marine torpedoes launched from surface ships or submarines.
[F42B 19/42] by releasing solid ballast -- F42B 19/42 concerns marine torpedoes that adjust their depth or trajectory by discarding solid ballast, allowing the weapon to change buoyancy and thus ascend, descend, or stabilize during its underwater run.
[F42B 19/44] by enlarging displacement -- The CPC entry F42B 19/44 covers methods of increasing the displacement of a marine torpedo, typically by enlarging its hull or internal volume to alter buoyancy, payload capacity, or range. It is classified under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive charges for marine torpedoes.
[F42B 19/46] adapted to be launched from aircraft -- The F42B 19/46 subclass concerns marine torpedoes that have been specially designed or modified for launch from aircraft, including launch tubes, release mechanisms, aerodynamic shaping and integration with airborne platforms. It pertains to torpedo systems adapted to be deployed by fixed-wing or rotary-wing aircraft, falling under the broader categories of mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive charges.
[F42B 21/00] Depth charges -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Anti-submarine warfare (ASW) weapon intended to destroy or cripple a target submarine by the shock of exploding near it. Most use explosives and a fuze set to go off at a preselected depth in the ocean. Depth charges can be dropped by either surface ships, patrol aircraft, or from helicopters.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Projectiles, missiles or mines characterised by the warhead, the intended effect, or the material -> F42B12/00
[F42B 22/00] Marine mines, e.g. launched by surface vessels or submarines -- **Definition:** This place covers:  A marine mine is a self-contained explosive device placed in water to destroy surface ships or submarines. Mines are deployed from ships, submarines and aircraft and usually left unattended until they are triggered by the approach of, or contact with, an enemy vessel. Marine mines can be used offensively-to hamper enemy shipping movements or lock vessels into a harbour; or defensively-to protect friendly vessels and create "safe" zones.  The main group is organised according to the functionality or the mine, the deployments aspects, tactical aspects and detail structural features.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Projectiles, missiles or mines characterised by the warhead, the intended effect, or the material -> F42B12/00
[F42B 22/02] Contact mines -- F42B 22/02 designates contact mines, which are maritime explosive devices that detonate upon physical impact with a vessel. These mines are typically deployed from surface ships or submarines and belong to the broader category of explosive charges used in mechanical engineering.
[F42B 22/04] Influenced mines, e.g. by magnetic or acoustic effect -- This CPC subclass covers marine mines that are triggered by external influences such as magnetic fields or acoustic signatures, including mines designed to detonate when detecting the magnetic signature of a vessel or specific sound patterns.
[F42B 22/06] Ground mines -- Ground mines are buried or otherwise concealed explosive devices designed to detonate when triggered by pressure, proximity, or remote command, providing anti-personnel or anti-vehicle protection in land warfare. The classification situates them within mechanical-engineering ammunition, specifically explosive charges such as marine mines, under CPC code F42B 22/06.
[F42B 22/08] Drifting mines -- F42B 22/08 covers drifting mines, a type of marine mine that is released from a vessel and allowed to float freely without anchoring or propulsion, relying on currents to carry it to a target area. These mines are employed for naval area denial and can be triggered by contact, magnetic, acoustic or pressure mechanisms.
[F42B 22/10] Moored mines -- Moored mines are underwater explosive devices anchored to the seabed and suspended at a set depth, designed to detonate on contact or by the influence of a passing vessel. They are deployed from surface ships or submarines for naval warfare and mine-countermeasure operations.
[F42B 22/12] at a fixed depth setting -- F42B 22/12 refers to marine mines that are equipped with a mechanism to detonate at a preset depth, such as mines launched from surface vessels or submarines. The subclass covers designs where the depth setting is fixed before deployment, ensuring the explosive charge activates when the mine reaches the designated underwater level.
[F42B 22/14] at a variable depth setting -- The subclass F42B 22/14 covers marine mines that are launched from surface ships or submarines and feature a variable-depth setting, allowing the device to sink to a selected depth before arming or detonating. This capability lets the mine target vessels with different drafts or operating depths, enhancing its effectiveness against a range of maritime threats.
[F42B 22/16] using mechanical means, e.g. plummet and float -- The code F42B 22/16 concerns marine mines whose deployment or positioning is achieved by mechanical devices such as plummets and floats, enabling the mine to sink to a preset depth or stay at a chosen level in the water and thereby control the explosive charge's action.
[F42B 22/18] using hydrostatic means -- The classification F42B 22/18 covers marine mines, launched from surface vessels or submarines, that are deployed, armed or triggered by hydrostatic means such as water pressure or fluid-filled devices. It pertains to ammunition in the explosive-charges group of mechanical engineering, specifically those using fluid pressure to control mine operation.
[F42B 22/20] using magnetic or acoustic depth-control means -- F42B 22/20 relates to marine mines that incorporate magnetic or acoustic devices to regulate their underwater depth. The subclass covers mines launched from surface vessels or submarines that use these sensors to maintain a preset depth, enhancing stability and effectiveness of the explosive charge.
[F42B 22/22] having self-contained sinking means -- This CPC subgroup (F42B 22/22) pertains to marine mines that include a self-contained sinking mechanism, allowing the mine to descend autonomously after being released from a surface vessel or submarine. The classification captures designs where the sinking means is integrated within the mine rather than provided by an external device.
[F42B 22/24] Arrangement of mines in fields or barriers -- F42B 22/24 addresses the design of minefields or barriers by specifying how naval mines are positioned and spaced to achieve desired coverage, detection avoidance and tactical effect, covering arrangements deployed from surface vessels or submarines.
[F42B 22/42] with anti-sweeping means, e.g. electrical -- F42B 22/42 covers marine mines designed to resist or defeat sweeping operations, typically by incorporating electrical anti-sweeping devices. The classification applies to explosive charges deployed from surface ships or submarines that include features to disrupt or disable minesweeping equipment.
[F42B 22/44] adapted to be launched from aircraft -- F42B 22/44 covers marine mines-explosive charges intended for use in naval warfare-that are specially adapted to be launched from aircraft. It falls under the mechanical-engineering branch of the CPC, within the ammunition and explosive-charges categories.
[F42B 23/00] Land mines -- **Definition:** This place covers:  A land mine is a self-contained explosive device placed on the ground or buried under the ground with the intention to destroy ground vehicles or harm infantrymen. Mines are deployed manually according to a very detailed planned pattern or scattered from minelaying vehicles of cargo ammunitions and usually left unattended until they are triggered by the approach of, or contact with, a vehicle or a person.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Projectiles, missiles or mines characterised by the warhead, the intended effect, or the material -> F42B12/00
[F42B 23/005] Selfpropelled land mines -- F42B 23/005 covers self-propelled land mines, which are explosive devices designed to move across terrain autonomously or by a propulsion system to locate and detonate against targets. These mines may incorporate wheels, tracks, or rocket propulsion and are classified under ammunition and explosive charges within mechanical engineering.
[F42B 23/04] anti-vehicle -- A land-mine type designed to incapacitate, damage or destroy moving vehicles, typically by detonating when a vehicle applies sufficient pressure or triggers a sensing mechanism. It falls under mechanical engineering ammunition for explosive charges, focusing on anti-vehicle applications.
[F42B 23/08] non-metallic -- F42B 23/08 designates non-metallic land-mine technologies within the CPC, covering explosive devices made from materials such as plastic, polymer, ceramic or composite substances rather than metal. The entry addresses design, manufacturing and functional aspects specific to these non-metal constructions, which fall under the broader categories of ammunition, explosive charges and land-mine engineering.
[F42B 23/10] anti-personnel -- F42B 23/10 concerns anti-personnel land mines, which are explosive devices intended to injure or kill individuals by detonating when triggered by pressure, tripwire or other activation methods. These mines fall under mechanical engineering, ammunition and explosive charges used for blasting, fireworks and ammunition.
[F42B 23/14] non-metallic -- F42B 23/14 designates land-mine casings or components that are formed from non-metallic materials such as plastics, composites or wood, including the shaping, joining and finishing of these parts for use in explosive charges. The subclass addresses manufacturing methods, material properties and protective features specific to non-metallic mine construction.
[F42B 23/16] of missile type, i.e. for detonation after ejection from ground -- F42B 23/16 refers to missile-type land mines that are engineered to be ejected from the ground and detonate after flight. The subclass falls under mechanical engineering > ammunition > explosive charges, describing devices that function like a missile rather than a buried charge.
[F42B 23/24] Details
[F42B 25/00] Fall bombs -- **Definition:** This place covers:  A conventional aircraft-delivered bomb that does not contain a guidance system and hence, simply follows a gravitationally imposed ballistic trajectory.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Means for influencing, e.g. improving, the aerodynamic properties of projectiles or missiles; Arrangements on projectiles or missiles for stabilising, steering, range-reducing, range-increasing or fall-retarding -> F42B10/00 - Projectiles, missiles or mines characterised by the warhead, the intended effect, or the material -> F42B12/00
[F42B 27/00] Hand grenades -- **Definition:** This place covers:  A conventional hand grenade is an explosive device with an explosive charge, an enclosing casing and a initiator arrangement, adapted to be thrown by hand. Traditionally the grenade has timing means to delay detonation until the grenade is sufficiently remote from the person throwing it. Embodiments with impact fuzes or the like are exceptions. Furthermore the casing of the grenade can be designed so as to create splinters upon detonation.  The explosive charge is meant to cause blast (offensive grenade) and/or spread fragments (defensive grenades).  This main group also includes non-lethal hand grenades or hand grenades with reduced lethality, such as flash-bang or stun grenades.  Finally smoke/gas/incendiary/illuminating grenades specially adapted to be thrown by hand are included in this main group.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Projectiles, missiles or mines characterised by the warhead, the intended effect, or the material -> F42B12/00
[F42B 27/08] with handle -- The code F42B 27/08 refers to hand-grenade designs that include a handle or grip to assist in throwing and aiming. It covers grenades where a stick-like or ergonomic handle is an integral part of the device, distinguishing them from grenades that are held directly in the hand. This subclass is part of the broader category for explosive charges used in ammunition.
[F42B 29/00] Noiseless, smokeless, or flashless missiles launched by their own explosive propellant -- The F42B 29/00 subclass pertains to missiles that are propelled by their own explosive charge and engineered to produce little or no noise, smoke, or flash, such as silent, smokeless or flashless projectiles used in ammunition and explosive-charge applications.
[F42B 3/00] Blasting cartridges, i.e. case and explosive -- **Definition:** This place covers:  Includes blasting cartridges per se, i.e. the assembly of an outer protecting case containing a predefined amount of explosive, ready for the use in blasting operation. Usually the case has a provision for inserting or attaching an initiator or detonator.  Further inlcudes initiators for the blasting cartridges and means for mounting the initator to the blasting cartridge.  Also included are detonation wave guides in blasting cartridges and explosive bolts.  Also includes means for initiation or detonation.  Includes gas generators and explosive bolts.  **Limiting references (this place does not cover):** - Detonation wave guides in hollow charges -> F42B1/024 - Fuse cords, e.g. detonating fuse cords -> C06C5/00 - Chemical aspects of detonators, blasting caps or primers -> C06C7/00 - Time fuzes -> F42C9/00 - Electric time fuzes -> F42C11/06 - Primers -> F42C19/08 - Relative timing of multiple charges -> F42D1/06
[F42B 30/00] Projectiles or missiles, not otherwise provided for, characterised by the ammunition class or type, e.g. by the launching apparatus or weapon used -- **Definition:** This place covers:  In this main group ammunition is mainly classified according to the launcher the ammunition is made to be launched from, i.e. bullets (for smallarms), grenades for launching form a rifle or specifically made for ordnance like mortars (larger calibre guns) and pyrotechnically launched (not hand held) harpoons. [F42B 30/14] Harpoons -- Harpoons are projectile weapons launched from a vessel or platform to penetrate, capture or damage a target, commonly employed in maritime and underwater operations such as fishing, naval combat or anti-submarine warfare.
[F42B 3/02] adapted to be united into assemblies -- F42B 3/02 defines blasting cartridges whose case and explosive charge are specifically designed to be joined with other elements-such as primers, detonators or additional propellant-to form a complete assembled cartridge for use in blasting, fireworks or ammunition. [F42B 3/045] Hybrid systems with previously pressurised gas using blasting to increase the pressure, e.g. causing the gas to be released from its sealed container -- The subclass covers blasting cartridges that incorporate a sealed container of pre-pressurised gas together with an explosive charge; when the charge detonates it ruptures the container, releasing and boosting the gas pressure. [F42B 3/087] Flexible or deformable blasting cartridges, e.g. bags or hoses -- Flexible or deformable blasting cartridges are pliable containers, such as bags or hoses, that hold explosive charges for blasting operations.
